A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash vane of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these yo ioifes are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were tonus payments—more than twiee the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

MEDLOW (Blue Mountains).
This charming Estate has its Railway Platform almost
adjoining, and its level land proves it to be eminently
adapted for residential purposes. Nature seems to have
lavished all h§r varied charms on BUCKINGHAM PARK,
which is, without exception, the CREAM or PICKED
SPOT on the BLUE MOUNTAINS.
BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE
Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large—from 100 up to 180 feet frontages, with long depths
—thus enabling buyers to have Garden, Tennis Lawns,
etc., if they so desire. In fact, it may be truly said, there
is no liner Residential Land on the Mountains.
. The BELGRAVIA HOTEL, MEDLOW, which cost £4OOO,
is now open for visitors, and is the First Hotel on the
Mountains supplied with the Electric Light, and the
Proprietor is willing to supply any cottages built on the
estate with the electric light. Two elegant Villas are
now nearly finished on this estate. The rapid advance in
the price of land at BLACKHEATH during the past three
years, amounting to fully 300 per cent., clearly shows that
early buyers at Buckingham Park will make a splendid
investment. Title is TORRENS, and the terms are liberal
and to suit buyers. For particulars, apply to the Pro-
prietor,

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
'‘Correspondence’’ column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 svords, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
“ The Bulletin’’ will return all unavailable MSS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.

can have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every iveek
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with ivhom %ve do not wish
to interfere.
SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 14, 1891.
Single-tax alias Confiscation.
Whenever “the creature Protection'’ begins
to loom on the New South Wales political
horizon, then does the Single-tax League
gird up its brains for the fray, and shove its
nostrum to the front. Then, too, do the
Sydney D.T. and the S.M. Herald, and the
rest of the shoddy press, begin to boom
Henry George’s doctrines ; and the theme
of their tirade is that the country should
give up Protection, and whoop instead for
the combined elements of Land Nationalisa-
tion and Foreign-trade. They never do any-
thing to bring the Single-tax policy any
nearer. They never even scream for that great
doctrine, except when Protection seems close
at hand, and then they applaud the noble
theory of a public revenue derived solely from
the land, and thereby induce the washy en-
thusiasts of the League to roll up and save
the importing interest from destruction.
And when they do this, and the danger is
thereby averted for the time, the Single-tax
gospel is forgotten, and the Foreign-trade
pi ess drops that creed in the waste-paper-
basket of oblivion, until it wants to make use
of its apostles at yet another political crisis.
Whereupon it cheers once more for the creed
of Henry George ; and the boneless, spine-
less, unreasoning fanatics whom it borrowed
and used and then threw overboard, roll up
again, to be used for Foreign-trade purposes
and thrown overboard as before.
i. # # *
! This policy is just now again in full
blast. With the advent of the Dibbs
Ministry, Protection is looming very near
at hand, and, as usual, the Telegraph
pnd the S.IVL Herald, and the Foreign-
trade party in general, all rise to scream for
the Single-tax gospel, in order to head off
the attack on the great importing monopoly.
None of them ever did or ever will do any-
thing to bring that reform about. No solitary
member of the Foreign-trade party desires
to see it come in his lifetime, or in that of
the most remote of his descendants. For
the Single-tax policy spells absolute ruin to
the land-owner ; and every individual who
has got money invested in freehold or lease-
hold property, in bank shares, building
Society shares, land companies, or any other
form of speculation even remotely connected
with land, will die in the last ditch before
golden age begins. The M‘Millans
and the Burdekiks and the rest of the
Foreign-trade capitalists, who vaguely profess
to sympathise with the great movement, will
fight behind a barricade, armed with guns
and hatchets, and the Herald and the Tele-
graph will call out the entire staff down to
the office-boy and the char-lady, and “ rcvo-
lushe ” in the highways when the Single-tax
fs really coming ; and even three-fourths of
the League itself will rather fill Sydney with
massacre than have any Single-tax at all.
For even Protection will not be one-tenth as
destructive to the rich monopolists of
Parke, s’ following as tho kind of Land
Nationalisation they profess to long for, and,
therefore, when tho fanatics of tlic League
prop up tho Foreign-trade interest under
the impression that the Foreign-trade in-
terest will prop them up in return, they
show a lamentable want of ordinary intelli-
gence. If they expected the Pope to go
forth and scream honestly and conscien-
tiously in an Orange procession, they would
thereby show themselves at least a shade less
ridiculous and less void of brains than they
are just now.
« » ss
At the present moment the Dibbs Ministry
proposes the adoption of. Protection as the
only means of saving the province from irre-
deemable insolvency. It is not expected to
yield any direct increase of revenue, for New
South Wales already pays as much under
its alleged “ Freetrade tariff ” as it is likely
to do under the most stringent system of
Protection ; but it will cause a general
revival of prosperity, and thereby bring about
an improvement in every branch of revenue.
Therefore, when the Single-tax League, and
the political party which drags the League
behind it, demand that revenue should bo
obtained from the land instead of from the
Custom-house, and that all taxation, except-
ing the land-tax, should be abolished, it is
only reasonable to ask that they should sub-
mit full particulars of their scheme. So far,
the League has never once gone into particu-
lars. It is a hazy organisation which deals
in hazy generalities. It is too weak or too
ignorant or too badly scared to define its
own programme. It demands that all the
revenue should be derived directly from the
land, but it never dares to face the conse-
quences of its own policy ; and it urges the
Dibbs Cabinet to do a thing which can only
be accomplished by bold, naked, and utterly
remorseless confiscation ; yet it lacks the
courage to preach confiscation on its own
responsibility. The Herald and the Tele-
grarh and the Foreign-trade press at large,
and the Single-tax leaguers in general have
all risen within the last few' days to denounce
the new Ministry for adopting Protectionist
instead of Single-tax principles, yet not
one of them has had the common
honesty to point out that if the Govern-
ment did adopt these vaunted princi-
ples, then it must steal in one act the
hard-worked agriculturist’s farm, and the
workman’s garden, and the squatter’s station,
and pay not one solitary cent of compensa-
tion to any one of them. And now many
ignorant individuals want to vote against the
Dibbs Government for not doing according
to the noble principles of the Telegraph and
the other papers ; yet if the Government
had acted according to these principles, the
same people would presently have desired to
hang the Dibbs Cabinet on the most adjacent
lamp-post.
* * *
The entire annual “ unimproved ” value of
all the lands of New South Wales, excepting
those unsold and unoccupied lands which
bring in no rent whatever, are, according to
Mr. Coghlan, a trifle less than £10,000,000
per annum. The entire expenditure of the
Government is also- a trifle less than
£10,000,000 per annum. Consequently, the
two items suit each other to perfection. The
idea of the League is that the rents of the
lands of the province should pay all the
expenses of Government, and there are just
sufficient of them to do it. Therefore, if the
State seizes upon all the lands of New South
Wales, and bounces out the present owners
without any compensation whatever, it will
just get enough, and no more than enough,
to run the country on the Single-tax plan.
If it pays compensation, how'ever, the scheme
will yield no present profit whatever. For, in
that case, the Government would have to
borrow, at least, £200,000,000 to buy out the
present owners, so as to nationalise the land
on a fair basis ; and supposing that gigantic
sum were obtained at 5 per cent. —which,
considering its magnitude, is utterly impos-
sible—the national accounts would stand
thus :
Expenditure.
Ordinary expenses of State as at present .. £10,000,000
Interest on loan for compensation of land-
holders .. .. .. 10,000,000
£20,000,000
Revenue.
sents5 ents v,. ■ • • ■ • • - £10,000,000
Customs duties, &c., as at present .. 10,000,000
£20,000,000
Therefore, if the present land-owners, many
of whom have bought their land from the
State, and paid for it by much toil—by
means, in fact, of their life-savings— are to
be compensated, instead of plundered by a
gigantic system of spoliation, there will be
exactly the same necessity for customs’
duties after the land is nationalised as there
is at the present moment.
* * *
It is because the Single-tax League has
never had the courage to face this problem,
but chooses to stand in the way of an honest
and practicable method of securing to the
State all unearned increment in the price of
land, that The Bulletix, denounces it as a
useless and insincere combination. For it
travels entirely on the glorious advantages to
be gained after the land has returned to the
possession of the State, and it poses as a
public benefactor and gains much applause
from the unthinking multitude by demanding
that the State should possess the land ; but
it always leaves to somebody else the odium
of bringing its millennium about. It
virulently abuses the new Protectionist
Government for not achieving a reform
which can only be achieved by a hideou 8
and utterly remorseless process of plun-
dering, but it never once dares to openly
and in as many words recommend that
process. There is a certain glorious
end to bo gained by utterly repulsive
means, and the League wants to let someone
else provide the means while it takes credit
for the end. And the /Herald and the
Telegraph and the Foreign-trade party in
general—which have attempted absolutely
nothing to bring the great reform about—
now howl bitterly because the Dices Govern-
ment does not do what they have also left
undone. If that Government could be in-
duced to attempt such a thing the mere fact
that the attempt was made by a Protectionist
Cabinet would make Protection impossible
for 20 years to come, and the instant the effort
was made the Foreign-trade press would
demand that the heads of the new Ministers
should be torn off by a furious mob, and
deposited on the Herald’s doorstep. It
screams because its opponents refuse to com-
mit suicide, but it has no visible intention of
suiciding itself by following out its own pro-
gramme.
* * *
The reason why the Foreign-trade party
does not want the Single-tax system— de-
spite the manner in which it uses the Single-
tax League for its own ends—is sufficiently
obvious. For if that system were adopted
then Sir Daniel Cooper would be stone-
broke, and Burdekin would be penniless,
and Bruce Smith would be burst up.
Then, too, the land on which the Herald
and Telegraph offices are built would be
forfeited ; and the great A.M.P. Society
would vanish into thin air, for most of its
money is invested in land and mortgages, all
of which would become of no value ; and the
big Colonial Mutual office, and the National
Mutual, and the Australian Widows’ Fund
would all explode for the same reason, and
all the people who have trusted to these
offices for a provision for their families would
be left in the lurch. Then, too, all the
building societies in which the poor man’s
little pile is planted would go bankrupt, for
their assets are almost all in land, and the
land would be confiscated by the State ; and
the savings-banks and the banks of issue
would collapse together ; and everything
would be chaos. The Foreign-trade party
wants no Single-tax policy ; and though
it uses the League as a convenient tool with
which to undermine Protection, it would
rise up and secretly murder the members of
that organisation in the night if there Avas
the slightest danger of their great scheme
ever becoming anything more than an empty
Avar-whoop and a convenient advertisement.
* * #
The Bulletin was a consistent advocate
of Land Nationalisation before the imbecile
Single-tax League Avas invented, and it will
still advocate it when that foolish organisa-
tion is buried and forgotten. But Land
Nationalisation is neither a Freetrade nor a
Protectionist policy, and it cannot be used
to bolster up any fiscal creed whatever.
Under its provisions the State Avill honestly
re-purchase—most probably by giving the
owners debentures in payment—the °land
which it sold in the past, and will thereafter
abolish all private ownership. This process
Avill yield no immediate return, for what (he
State receives in rent it will pay out in
interest, but all future increase of value will
be the property of the nation. In the mean-
time, however, the necessity for revenue
will remain just the same Avith Land
Nationalisation as Avithout it, and even when
a vast income has grown up Avhich will
render all ordinary taxation superfluous, the
demand for a Protective tariff will still exist.
No country can live on Land Nationalisation
alone ; if Asiatic and cheap European pro-
ducts are admitted free, then Avages will sink
to the Asiatic or cheap European level just
the same, Avhether the land belongs to the
State or to the private landlord. But
the Single - tax League has chosen to
boom Land Nationalisation as a Freetrade
policy. It demands that all taxation
shall be abolished save the land-tax only,
and to do this the State must seize upon all
the rent of all the land, which is robbery
pure and simple. There is no possibility of
compensation, for then the Government
Avould require to retain all the existing
sources of revenue to pay interest on its
gigantic debt, and in that case there Avould
be no Single-tax, but just the same number
of taxes that there are at present. I here-
fore, if the Single-tax League possesses the
courage of its convictions, it will honestly
preach direct confiscation and take the con-
sequences, Avhatever they may be. At pre-
sent it is carrying on a crusade against the
Protectionist Government because it will not
do that which the League lacks the courage
even to say, and the position is a despicable
one for any league to occupy.
Subpœenæring the Lord.
Has the present Dibbs Ministry a sufficient sense
of justice, and courage enough to promptly
acknowledge and atone, as far as now may be,
for the ghastly error committed in regard to
the Mount Rennie case a few years ago?
Henry Copeland, Minister for Lands, stood up
the other evening in the New South Wales
Assembly to justify Governor Cakinoton’s
share in the blasphemy against justice which
terminated in the strangling of four boys found
guilty on the oath of a complaisant little street-
marcher, backed up by the testimony of two
men— Stanley, since distinguished as the
utterer of a forged cheque; and Smith, a dis-
graced jockey, now doing penal servitude for
arson. When Mr. Copeland spoke he spoke
not for Gov, Carinqton alone, but he held a
brief for himself. Said he, with gore-champing
jaws:— 1 ®
I find, in the 20th chapter of Judges, that, for ravishing
one woman, no less than 22,000 of the Children of Israef
were, by direction of the Lord, slain in one day • is ntii
wore slain on the second day; and, on the third fin,,
25,000 of the Children of Benjamin were slain ; making
altogether 65,000 of the Jewish people slain, by direction
of the Lord, because one woman was ravished.
In other words, Mr. Henry Copeland would
saddle the Lokd with the perpetration of the
Mount Rennie gallows-murders. Was the
Lord also responsible for the damnable brutali-
ties pf their trial, for the shameless parody on
human justice that staked the lives of youths
against the physical endurance of counsel and
jurors, with the make-weight thrown in of the
personal convenience of the Judge ? Can Cope-
land, who was then a Minister of the Crown
even with the help of the Lokd get
over the hideous central facts of the case?
Putting aside all formed opinions of innocence
or guilt—was the trial of the Mount Rennie
scapegoats a fair one? Was it not a blood-
seeking insult to the democracy ? Consider the
following facts only—wc ask members to follow
us closely here—to the exclusion of all others
and give an answer:—The Court commenced
the case at 10 o’clock on a Monday morning
sitting right on till about 9.30 in the evening-
on Tuesday it sat from 9 a.m. till past 11.30
P-m. ; on Wednesday (a night on which pre-
siding Judge Windeyek dined with Sir Robert
Fowler, an alleged “distinguished visitor’’from
Hingland) it sat from 9 a.m. till only 7 p, m .
on Thursday, ditto (his Honor on this night
dining at Government House in company with
the same potentate) ; on Friday-oura-Saturday
the Court sat right on from nine o'clock on
Friday morning until after four a.m. on Satur-
day viorning, the Judge alleging that he did
not wish to keep the jury locked up over the
Sunday (!) ; the Court then again met at nine
a.m. on the, same day (!), the Judge summed up
for ten solid hours, during which from time to
time some of the jurymen were comatose, and
the verdict which fed victims to the gallows
was given a little before the midnight that
ushered in the holy Sabbath. Mark how the
prisoners’ counsel were placed. Had they done
more than record their protest against the
enforced exhaustion of all concerned as being
monstrous, they would have provoked the ill-
will of jurymen who had been locked up every
night for a week, and who would have
debited counsel for the defence with being the
unwarranted cause of their incarceration over
Sunday. Do these circumstances strike Cope-
land as having been “by direction of the
Lord ” ? If he takes that line of argument, he
has a wide range of atrocities to applaud, ’ It
will be within his rule to excuse—nay, to advo-
cate—as justifiable concomitants of war, but-
cheries similar to those perpetrated by the
Israelites on the boy-captives of Midian,
besides the personal appropriation,
and enslavement of the maidens, “ by direction
ot the Lord f per the mouth of Moses ; or like
to the utter destruction of the subjects of Og,
King of Bashan, “ men, women, and children ” ;
or the burning of Jericho and the massacre of
its inhabitants, “ both man and woman, young
and old, and ox, and sheep, and ass. with the
edge of the sword ” ; or the similar devilry at
Ai and the hanging of its king, and five other
kings, all military prisoners; and—but why con-
tinue the list of deeds of supersatanic rapine and
wholesale atrocities committed by the victorious
fiends who wiped out the Canaaniter, “ by
direction of the Lord ” ? Mr. Copeland ought
to carry his bull-headed, Yorkshire-bred
scriptural enthusiasm further, and bring in
bills to stone to death adulterous women ;to
enforce the quittance of “ an eye for an eye and
a tooth for a tooth ”; for the legalisation of
slavery, “as directed by the Lord”; for the
recognition of polygamy‘and harems of concu-
bines ; and many other sweet and ancient
practices. He didn’t, moreover, give the pre-
liminary passages of the incident in which his
heart found justification for the Mount Rennie
gallows-massacre ; he ought to have told how a
pious Israelite offered his own virgin daughter
as a sacrifice to the lewdness of the “sons of
Belial, and he might have founded thereupon
a theme on which to lavish all the laudation
due to a strictly law-conforming religiqnist,
who was also an inhumanly unnatural father.
Also, he might have made a point of the fact
that, whereas the champions of the ravishers,
the children of Benjamin, only lost 25,000
men, the revengers of outraged virtue, on their
side, lost 40,000—a proportion which ought to
be a lesson to immoderate zeal in bloodthirsti-
ness. Mr. Copeland does not shine in his
assumption of legal partnership with the Lord.
Unless the Dibbs Ministry do something for
the remaining Mount Rennie miserables, or
show cause—which we challenge them to do-
why something should not be done, they will
yet lose a lot of votes in a close division on
which their political life depends. They were
responsible for the Soudan act of brigandage,
and the public are now just as ashamed of the
legal massacre perpetrated under Dibbs and
Co.’s mgis, as it is at the Australian invasion
of the Soudan.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Nautical Sky-Pilots.
Dear Bulletin, —
The man who objects to another man’s fad, when it is
thrust upon him, is distinctly justified.—Sydney Bulletin,
October 30, 1891.
The foisting of public religious services, generally
Church of England, by a few cranks upon un-
fortunate and unsuspecting passengers at sea, 1
is becoming an intolerable nuisance. As a rule,
the chief offender is a bigoted captain who has
mistaken his vocation, and is fond of filling up
unwilling yet helpless passengers—for it would
be considered bad form to object publicly —
with his own dreary ctbgmas and dissertations.
Why should people of various sects, likewise
the constantly-swelling numbers who belong to
no sect whatever, be subjected, nolens volens, to
a discourse from a narrow-minded seaman or
clergyman, or from some smug layman who
loves to show off? In one mail steamer


I travelled by, there was a Church of England
service, in the first-class saloon, every morn-
ing at 11. On Sundays there were two—
at 11 a.m. and 7 p.m. The saloon was mono-
polised for the time being by this joyous sect,
and you had either to attend their service or
clear out. An exasperated passenger at last
posted a notice upon the companion-way board,
requesting “that those who wished to pray,
would not interfere with those who wanted to
read or write ; and, further, that the ship
Would not be turned into a floating church.”
This was rough, but the impervious preacher
went on just the same. One Sunday evening
a disturbance took place in the second
saloon. The Church of England service
was being performed there, and a young man
refused to leave off writing, but removed his
materials out of the way to the far end table.
At the conclusion of the services the intolerant
devotees, fresh from their orisons, cordially
hissed him. Several first-class passengers in
the smoking-room, myself among the number,
went to see what the matter was, and upon my
expressing an opinion that the young man was
fully justified some fanatics almost tore the
coat off my back. For an instance near home,
I was travelling recently from Wellington
(M.L.) on a rough Sunday evening, 24 hours
out, with seven-eighths of the passengers pro-
strated with sea-sickness in their bunks, and
trying to get a little sleep. Yet the captain
waded through the service, as usual, and threw
in a long and incoherent exhortation of his own,
and as his stentorian voice roused up one weary
invalid after another, the spoken and unspoken
curses that were heaped on his head were
enough to sink any ordinary preacher. Rational
freethinkers, agnostics, and others of advanced
thought (or, if you like, diseased thought—for
this isn’t an essay on religion, but on manners)
almost always respect the feelings and the pre-
judices of conscientiously religious people, hold-
ing that none can say dogmatically what
theories are right or vice versa, but the aggra-
vated and incurable soi-disant Christain never
does. What I would suggest is, that a ballot
should be taken among the passengers to decide
if there should be Sunday service on board,
and if so, of what denomination —though
even this idea would be written down
as blasphemy by many Christians I know.
Meanwhile, many passengers to and from
Europe, with the view of escaping the dreadful
British Sunday-at-sea, are patronising the
French and German steamers,' where they
know rational treatment can be depended upon.
In the average British steamer a Jew or an
agnostic or a Catholic must either sit and listen
every Sunday to doctrines that he repudiates,
or else go overboard—it is impossible to get out
of range by any other means. It is not much
that they ask—only that no captain, or other
fanatic, should be allowed to seize them in the
middle of the Pacific, and preach them to death
where there is no room for escape. —Yours,
&c., J.B.
[This quite fits in with the experience of many people.
“J.B.’s” grievance is a genuine one. The Church of
England appears to be Established on boaid some of the
mail-steamers.—Ed. B.]
■ ♦
Unproductive Works.
New South Wales Premier Dibes’ promise to
construct unproductive public works for the
future out of revenue, instead of out of loan-
moneys, has raised a yell of indignation in the
columns of the Sydney Daily Telegraph.
There is nothing distinctly objectionable about
the idea of borrowing only for works that will
pay expenses ; it is admittedly a thing that
ought to have been done from the beginning,
and in any case the simple policy of having a
high old time to-day and leaving the bill to
posterity is about played out; but the frantic
Foreign-trade paper feels that the proposal
is a reflection on Parkes’s past doings, and, any-
how, everything that Dibes says or does must
be jumped on as a matter of principle. fhere-
fore, it gets up with a shriek to declare that no
money has ever been spent on unproductive
works in New South Wales, which assertion is
a large order, and shows that the paper that
makes it is capable of asserting anything. Up
to date the Frectrade province has sunk
£775,000 of British money on defence-works,
and if its moth-eaten fortifications and tottering
ramparts are a reproductive or saleable asset,
or a thing that pays any sort of dividend, then
it would be interesting to know where the
dividend comes from. Another £600,000 of
borrowed cash has gone in roads and bridges ;
they yield no profits, and they are also unsale-
able, for no one can tear up a road and trade it
off bv auction. They were constructed
in a great measure to enhance the value
of public lands, and when the value was
enhanced the Foreign-trade Governments
of the past sold the lands and “ blewecl
the proceeds royally, and left the Dill
for the roads and bridges unpaid. About
£400,000 more went in immigration —in import-
ing cheap paupers and human refuse ; if this is
a reproductive item, then the dictionary has
gone mad and the British language is oft its
head. Two millions has been spent (most of it
foolishly) on impossible sand-barred harbours,
and on river improvements ; this item is merely
repairs to the highways of the country, and is
no more a reproductive asset than the filling in
of a hole in a road ; it yields no dividends, and
it isn’t saleable, for a harbour can’t be dug up
and put in the market. Another £1,600,000
has departed to pay for public buildings which
produce no returns whatever, and which can t
be realised upon unless the public departments
are to be fired into the middle of the street;
and nearly £3,000,000 additional has gone in
discounts and per-centages to financiers an
item which can no more be charged to loan ex-
penditure as a reproductive asset than can the
interest on the national debentures, for it is
merely a dead and irrecoverable loss, faking
everything into account, therefore, fully
£8,000,000 has been borrowed in New South
Wales for unproductive purposes, and the
reason why the blatant Telegraph complains
so bitterly is because .Premier Dibes promises
not to add any more to this unprofitable load.
No doubt, if Dibbs arose and stated in a bald-
headed way that he was going to borrow
largely for unprofitable works the Telegraph
would howl and travel around on its ear just
the same. For so ,r > time past that paper has
published twelve leading-articles per week
against the new Protectionist Government
which proposes to live within its income because
the borrowing game is “up”—and its excite-
ment is a truly awful thing to look at.

Wales’s Birthday.
Wales had a birthday last Monday, and the
British Empire celebrated the event in five
continents and several dozen islands, ’there
was nothing surprising in the fact of Wales
having a birthday ; every other man has one
just as regularly as he does. There was nothing
commendable about it, for the little heir-
apparent didn’t make his birthday. There was
no cause to whoop about it, either, for it only
meant that Wales was 12 months older and 12
months nearer being dead than he was last birth-
day, and it is unkind to celebrate over the fact
that any man is getting nearer to the dusty tomb.
Moreover Wales’s mother is Head of the
Church, and Wales himself, if he lives long
enough, will be Head of the Church in his turn,
and the Church is solid on the subject of hell ;
and if that institution is correct in its views
then that is still another reason why it is a
base and ungenerous thing to rejoice over the
heir apparent’s approaching decease from old
age. Meanwhile the embryo monarch is a
dreary spectacle, and not by any means a good
subject to rejoice about. He has one dismal
eye fixed on his unpaid bills, and the
other on a ballet-girl in tights. He never
said anything brilliant, or did anything
surprising. The monarchy lives on the memory
of the old, brawny, mail-clad kings of past
history, but when its traditions get hitched on
to a little man with a heavy jowl, and a mean-
ingless expression, and a big stomach, and a
bald-head, • and small, uncertain legs, and a
gambling-kit in his portmanteau, it is rough on
the ancient legend. The world believes a great
deal in glitter and show, and the king who
waves a sword in the front of the battle can
rouse a lot of enthusiasm, but it is hard
work to enthuse about the potentate who
only waves a fork at a dinner - or
a big trump at a card - party. Even
a new Shakespeare couldn’t raise a poenj
or a drama about British royalty now-
adays, for pie doesn’t lend itself to
dramatic effect. You can make a brilliant
tragedy out of the monarch who perishes on the
field of battle, but you can’t make any literary
effect out of the one who is in bed with gout
and varicose veins. The thing is as much out
of place as a bun would be if it were used as a
national standard; it is as incongruous as the
idea of an army rushing to battle with a wild
yell of “boiled ham!” as its war-cry. The
monarchy which rallies round a little, fat,
dismal person, with pig’s-eyes is doomed. All
the poetry and romance have gone out of it,
and the world wants poetry and romance. Its
ideas of what constitutes poetry may be limited,
but it wants the thing all the same. The ancient
church realised this fact ; it was watered by
the blood of the martyrs, and they looked poetic
at all events, therefore the Church prospered.
But if these old martyrs had died through over-
eating themselves, as the monarchy is dying
now, there would never have been any Church
worth mentioning at all. A course of vigorous
exercise, and a long series of Turkish baths,
might postpone the downfall of the Guelphs
by making them appear a shade more like what
fancy has painted a king to be, but even this is
only postponing the evil day. The House of
Brunswick is being shoved to destruction by its
own stomach, and its short, uncertain legs are
its death warrant.

Through All Eternity.
Rudyard Kipling, that brilliant literary man
from India, landed the other day on the coast
of Maori land, and straightway the “special
correspondent” of the Otago Witness went
forth and interviewed him. The following
remarks are part of the stuff with which the
visitor filled up the Witness man, and they
are memorable because they are the first words
which Kipling uttered in an official capacity
in the Southern Hemisphere. He had come
Hi,ooo miles and more, and his very first effort
on getting to land was to throw a hunk of
literature off his chest concerning the eternal
greatness of the awful British Empire :
“ England cannot colonise India,” he said. “ The pure
white race always dies out in three generations. I he
only chance of survival is intermarriage with the natives,
and then you t;ct a race that can stand the climate. The
pure Eii'dish blood cannot. India will always he hound
up with England. The colonies may grow into new
nations and separate in time, but India never.”
Think of that ! The Englishman dies out in
India in three generations, therefore, the con-
quering race can’t even get a footing there.
The only way is to make a hybrid nation, half
English, half Hindoo. These half-castes ate
born in India, and have Indian sympathies,
and intermarry with the native races, and they
know nothing of England, and care less but
all the same, India will belong to England for
ever and ever. For Rudyard Kipling, that
typical John Bull, has looked into futurity
far as human eye can see, and his
nation alone among the nations is going to
last for all time and all eternity. r lhe Last
Man is going to be an Englishman —that final
being who will stand his family umbrella up
against the blackened stars and the extinguished
sun, and hang his hat on the worm that never
dies, and keep his eye on the seraphim when
they suspend this old, dead, fire-blasted world
in th.e heavens to dry. He is a creature of
yesterday, a mere ephemeral insect, a fly stand-
ing on a leaf and giving advice to the Lord ,
his empire started just JOO years ago, and he
has already lost the richest half of it, and he
now dodders feebly on the half that remains,
and wails about his own everlastingness. Of
that remaining half, too, 75 per cent is prac-
tically gone, for Australia is so far on the road
to independence that she only receives orna-
mental Governors on sufferance, and Britain
dare not plant even one private soldier in this
land to show that it belongs to her ; and Canada
already talks freely of secession. The Musco-
vite, too, is looming very large at the
gates of India, and the Asiatic himself will
not remain subject for ever. Five times
already within the historic ages he has
swamped Europe, aiul half the men who now
call themselves Europeans are Asiatic con-
querors whose fathers ate up the original races,
and annexed their lands, and stole their
Avives and daughters, and generally took
over the inheritance of Japhet. But,
though the loss of one-half the empire may
reasonably suggest what is likely to become of
the other half, and though the great British
monarchy has been visibly falling to pieces ever
since that brief yesterday when it first began,
the faith of John Bull in his own eternal
durability is unshaken. He rises superior to
facts and dates and circumstances, and he opens
himself and lets his famous flag gush out of his
entrails at every possible opportunity. The
duration of his monarchy, compared to that of
the earth on which it stands, is as a grain of
sand beside a star ; he is a mere speck of dust
using bad language to a sun, but he is incapable
of realising his own smallness. The salutation
of the Egyptian people to their old Egyptian
kings was “ Pharaoh lives for ever;” the
whoop of the Englishman of to-day is “John
Bull lives for ever,” and he gets there just the
same. For nowadays the Pharaoh is dug out
of his grave and his remains are sold to a circus,
and where he has gone there John Bull will
go, and the learned African commentator of
8000 years hence will probably go blind with
looking through a microscope for Bull’s dust,
and will find it not.

A well-advertised “ Single - tax ” potentate,
William Affleck, writes to Sydney D.T. to
show how New T South Wales can get through
without Protection; and how it may yet
achieve that glorious destiny wdien its workers
must either accept wages on the Chinese or
European pauper scale—or else sec their indus-
tries extinguished by foreign competition :
Sir, — The newspapers report Mr. Lyne has given as a
reason why the Government will go to the Custom-house
instead of the land for revenue is the difficulty and loss
of time in collecting it. I do not think this a good
excuse, as it might be imposed to a certain extent without
any delay. Mix Cogolax has given out that the unim-
proved value of the alienated land of the colony in April
last, above one aero in extent, according to the census
was £205,000,C00. Let circulars bo issued from all the
lands offices ... to all those making their returns
by the census, taking their own value until a valuation
can be made, chanjimj 1 per cent fur a start, and we have
a revenue of £2,050,000 without any delay, as it could be
made payable by January 1 next. In the meantime
valuators could be appointed to value all the lands not
included in the return —viz., those lands under la. in
extent, which means city, town, and suburban lands, and
then the 1 per cent, tax could be assessed on these also.
. . . I think it can be seen by these few remarks Mr.
Lyxe’s excuse for not going to the lands for revci ue is
not a very substantial one. A land-tax we must have,
and the sooner the better, and if the new Ministry are
anxious to retain office they will lake the hint herein
given and never nlind tiro Custom-house, but begin at
once on the owner’s own value for a start.
Just so ! Then there will, we presume, be, a
little later on, no customs duties at all, and all
the revenue will come from the land. Then
the great wine-industry will collapse in one
act, and the brewing and distilling industry,
and the tobacco business, and the dairy produce
trade, for they are all kept up chiefly by Pro-
tection in an alleged Freetrade country. And
on top of this disaster there will be a charge of
1 per cent, per annum on the capital value of
land, equal to over £2O in every £lOO on the
annual rental, a tax which will come down with
a dull, sickening thud just when the country is
dying of too much Freetrade. And as this is only
“for a start,” the result will be immediate
panic, followed by the results to which
we have alluded elsewhere. Land National-
isation is coming right enough ; but no-
body save a remorseless idiot, with one cast-
iron fad, could suggest preparing for the change
by burying the national industries in general
ruin. Yet that is exactly what the single-tax
man wants, and ho wants it simply because his
dry-nurse, Henry George, lias told him to
want it.

As a hint to the New South Wales unemployed,
a Sydney D.T. writer remarks that nearly
£ll,OOO worth of sausage-skins are imported
yearly into New South Wales. The Sydney
slaughter-house offal, he points out, is thrown
away to make room for cheap, imported entrails
from other lands. Gracious benefactor ! TheNevv
South Wales workman is to support Foreign-
trade, give up all decent artisan-ship to the
people of distant countries, and then he is to
go to work cleaning pigs’ intestines and make
them into £ll,OOO worth of sausage-skins. New
South Wales under the Calico Jimmy regime
imports everything else, and, so far as we can
see, it might as well import the sausage-skins
as well. Why doesn’t the D.T. get excited
over some cleaner job than this ? If the province
isn’t to have Protection in the matter of rickety
Baldwin engines and things like that, it doesn't
want its offal protected. Or, at all events, it
doesn’t want to start by protecting offal. It
is just like a Foreign-trade paper to begin turn-
ing its coat by wanting to help the local in-
dustry in—pardon the plain Saxon—glorified
guts. The sort of country that the Sydney
D.T. syndicate wants is a country which lives
by cleaning tripe, while it buys everything
else ready-made.
The S.M. Herald, for some inscrutable reason,
allows one J. W. Hill to write in its columns
upon the tariff question. That individual is a
splendid type of the Foreign-trader of the
period. He begins, as usual, by a solid curse at
the Dibbs Government for wanting to bring in
Protection, and then he proceeds to tell that
combination exactly what it ought to do
instead ;
Why will the people not settle upon the land is surely
the question a solution of which would bring a peace and
plenty that would banish fiscal nostrums from us ? Is it
that young Australia is developing a new disease to be
known as terraphobia ? Then let us ship him off for some
of England’s sturdy yeomen. Is it the mania of Govern-
ments for centralising? Then let us insist upon a rigid
decentralisation. Is it that the land is too dear, or the
people have no money to start upon, or available land is
beyond the reach of cheap freights? Then why not give
the land rent free for long periods? There is surely
enough of it, lend the money at low rates and long terms.
The security on improvements and the property is good
enough, and if necessary, build them railways, the pro-
spective business would justify it. Why not do all this,
and more, if required, to settle the people upon the soil ?
In other words : (1) Reduce railway rates,
and thereby cut down the railway revenue
(which even now isn’t enough to pay interest
and expenses), and thus create another huge
shortage ; (2) give away the land free, and
thus extinguish the land revenue—these two
items alone will make a healthy additional
deficiency of about £2,000,000 per annum ;
(3) lend a lot of money—when there isn’t
any to lend —with no security, at low in-
terest, and lose both the principal and the
interest; (4) build more railways, when there
isn’t a cent to build them with, and also “ do
more if required.” Then go in for decentralisa-
tion. This is a large and blessed-sounding
word, and a vast number of politicians travel
on it and live on it, yet none of them ever
explain it much. In a general way, however,
it means that all the public offices shouldn’t be
concentrated in Sydney, but that about
£1,000,000 of Government premises should be
built all over the country, so that the work
may be scattered throughout the land. Truly
the world is choke-full of lunatics, and most of
them seem to write to the S.M, Herald.
The people of British Honduras, West Indies,
have curry com bed Lord Knutsforu the wrong
way. Honduras is imperially run by an “ Ad-
ministrator,’’ and the Legislature takes the
form of a Court, partly nominated and partly
elected. That Court recently decided that the
colony was not liable for the amount of a claim
sent in by a contractor. Knutsford insisted
that the claim should be paid. Whereupon the
elected members resigned, and the constitu-
encies declined to fill up the vacancies. Knuts-
ford ordered the Administrator to fill up the
places with nominees. Which was done.
The new Legislature imposed fresh taxes,
amongst other increases, raising the tobacco-tax
from 10 cents to 50 cents per pound. This put
the State “ tatur all on cend at getting biled,”
and public measures were taken accordingly.
Backed up by public subscriptions, a business
firm imported a lot of tobacco, paid the new duty
under protest, and then appealed to the Supreme
Civil Court. Chief Justice Anderson judicially
declared that all legislation passed by a Court
(meaning what Australians term “ the legisla-
ture ”) not strictly constituted according to
law— i. e., not composed partly of elected mem-
bers—was invalid. This judgment will pro-
bably be further referred to the Privy Council,
but, if that body correctly interprets the law,
it must be upheld. If not, still the Honduras
Custom House can’t very well enforce its tariff
in the interim. Knutsford has, meanwhile,
to call for a step-ladder to enable him to climb
down from his perch. Great Britain, with all
its vaunted power, cannot coerce its smallest
dependency against the will of the colonists.

Choice extract from a sermon by Rev. W.
Gray Dixon, of Warrnambool (Vic.) cursing
the principle of One-man-one-vote :
An Imperial Governor stood face to face with a mass
meeting of citizens. No ordinary Governor, and no
ordinary citizens. He represented Home, the mistress of
the world ; they represented Israel, the divinely-appointed
nation of free men. And the occasion, too, was no
ordinary occasion They were discussing a fellow-man,
whose personality everyone admitted to be phenomenal,
whose personality the civilised world has since acknow-
ledged to be co-equal with “ the great Gou Himself.”
Imperial authority on the one side, manhood suffrage on
the other—resultant, the destiny of “the Man who is
God.” Imperial authority nit ads for mercy, acquits of all
guilt; manhood suffrage cries for swift and utter punish-
ment. Imperial authority hesitates, asks for reasons;
but manhood suffrage, the more vehemently, if possible
the more unanimously, clamours tor vengeance. “ They
cried out the more, saying, ‘ Let Him be crucified. And
manhood suffrage prevails. Jesus is consigned to the
cross. The vote was a true vote. It was fully character-
istic, fully deliberate. It was the vote, not of a class,
but of a nation, and it was recorded in the broad
light of day, and with sufficient opportunity,
even urgently enforced, for reconsideration. In no
completer way could the will of mankind have been
learned that day— the will of mankind in its best develop-
ment. If the children of Abraham, par excellence,
humanity's free men, failed to vote straight, who else
could? That vote, “Let Him be crucified,” twice re-
peated, was a true vote-national, racial, personal. It
was “ One-man-one-volo” in its purest form. Property
had no say in it. It was personality that spoke. The
will of man as man—the unrestricted suffrage of manhood
cast the die that sent the Saviour to the Cross.
There is much solid reasoning of the bull-
headed parsouical variety in this. The people
of Palestine, 18 centuries ago, voted that
Christ should be executed, therefore the people
shouldn’t have a vote. The parsons of that day
voted louder and harder than all the rest—in
fact, it was their villainous plot from the very
beginning ; therefore the parson of to-day
shouldn’t have a vote either, and Gray-Dixon
shouldn't have one. The old inhabitants of
Judea were- judged by their actions a
ruffianly crowd who, couldn’t be trusted with
the suffrage, and from this Dixon infers, that
the inhabitants of Australia to-day must be
ruffianly and not lit to be trust el either. The
preachers of Jerusalem were te.i times more
villainous and bloodthirsty than the people ;
eri/o, the preacher of to-day—Gray-Dixon in-
cluded —is a bloodthirsty villain also for exactly
the same reason as applies to the people. If
the conduct of the Hebrew artizau in the year
33 or thereabouts proves that the artizans of
to-day arc a had lot, then the demand of the
old Hebrew clergyman that the Lord should
be crucified, proves that the modern clergyman
— i. e., Gray-Dixon—wants to crucify Him also.
If he wants such a thing, then we presume he
ought to be in gaol. Why isn’t Gray-Dixon in
gaol, then? If that preacher will only look at
the thing according to his own logic, he ought
to be doing about 2500 years penal servitude.
Why on earth does Jack Murray, M.L.A. and
Republican, allow such a person as Gray-Dixon
to go unmuzzled in bis electorate?
Reflection from “ Los Carritos,” a new book
by Gertrude F. Atherton :
Has it ever occurred to you that the rich are at the
mercy of the poor, not the poor at that of the rich? Who
permits us to be rich if not the poor? Who, generation
after generation, have slowly and inevitably forged the
conditions which allow' a man to amass w'ealth at the
expense of Iris brethren, hut the poor—since they repre-
sent the great body of humanity ? Supposing each man
renting a house in San Francisco should suddenly arise
and say, “ I will pay no more rent.” Who would compel
him? The police? Militia? They are included in the
renting community. Who would compel them? The
army? Supposing the epidemic spread? What chance
would the army have against the concerted millions of
America? But that is just where we are safe. There
never will be a concerted movement. The timid and the
indifferent element are too largely in the majority, and all
must take the stand or none at all. But the fact remains
that we rich men hold our wealth because the poor man
fosters the conditions which enable us so to do.
This is a solemn reflection when it is carefully
considered. The whole race of rack-renting
landlords, monopolists, usurers, emperors, mili-
tary tyrants, priests, and other oppressors
could be destroyed in one act if the world would

80 & 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand thajb ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*

only do it. There would be no necessity for
Woodshed—all that is required is that the
human race should take no notice of them.
When two inonarchs declare war, if their armies
would simply look at them with a dull, fish-like
eye and do nothing, then these potentates
are both abolished there and then. If the whole
race of tenants would only stare with a clammy
expression at the rack-renting landlord when lie
wants the rent, and would look with a petrified
glare at the usurer when he tries to collect his (50
per shent, and take no further notice of either of
them, then they are done for on the instant.
The huge, overbearing church which has grown
up slowly in the course of nineteen centurjes
could be wiped out in seven days or less if the
people would only sit up straight and refuse to see
anything when the plate comes round. Nearly
everything can be abolished, in fact, by passing
a unanimous resolution that it isn’t there, and
acting up to the resolution. There is no neces-
sity to clamour or howl against anything if the
world only knew it; all that is needed is to
wear a soulless, expressionless front and sit
down in a simultaneous way, and let it slide.

oolumn of Melb. Age :
Up to now whisky has been the only stuff that

Augustus Moore tells of a delightful
criticism which a French lady once passed on M.
Renan’s “ Vie de Christ.” She read the book
through with continually increasing interest, until
she reached the passage where the Saviour is
represented as looking back, on his way to
Calvary, upon those few girls of Galilee, one of
whom might, under other circumstances, have
made his bliss. Then she closed the volume with a
sigh. “Helas!” she said, “ what a pity the story
does not finish with a wedding !”
There died at Port Arthur, the other day,
Host Cowen, of the Carnarvon Hotel—the former
residence of the “ Commandants,” of gory
memory. Cowen was a remarkable man in his
way, having been captain of a Confederate frigate
in the American Civil War, and a lieutenant of
volunteers in the last Maoriland trouble. In his
time he visited nearly every habitable part of the
globe. His last place of residence ere he settled
i n t Tasmania was Fiji, and he was contemplating
a “shift” to South America when he died from
the effects of a bullet-wound in the head received
years ago in one of the numerous engagements in
which he took part. These were also responsible
for the fearsome scars that covered his body. He
was an artist of no mean ability.
Thus Parson Nye, of Bendigo :
Young man, I ask if you, with an arm round the waist
of a girl, and indulging in a waltz, can truthfully say you
are representing your Master ?
Also —
Christ, when upon earth, engaged in the trade of a
carpenter. It was quite competent for Him to have
engaged in any other business, but can you picture to
yourselves a public-house with the sign over the door—
“ Jesus Christ, Licensed to Sell Spirituous Liquors ?”
Why does the parson ask—
You flesh-loving married man, can you imagine Christ
married and carrying around His latest bahy ? You know
you cannot. Then, if you marry and propagate offspring
can you truthfully say you are representing Him ? ’
But a truce to sermonising. What is a highly
creditable Christian onslaught in Parson Nye is
rank blasphemy in a writer who wears a striped
necktie.
One of the boy writers of Melbourne
Standard, which has become demoralized by
associating with a sporting paper, proposes that
the local garrotters shall be put upon “ a course of
diet just sufficient to keep body and soul together,”
and “treated to occasional doses of the lash,”m
public. It is a juvenile Tory doctrine that the
only way to wipe brutality out of the land is to
supply society with brutal exhibitions gratis. On
the strength of this idea free hangings flourished
like a green bay tree until quite recently, and
murderers were introduced as shocking examples
to the notice of audiences who were mostly crack-
ing nuts. Why any newspaper should seriously
advise a revival of the old order of things is im-
possible to understand. Under present conditions
a possible garrotter can shudder over his penn’orth
of blood-curdling details in the evening rag,
whereas a practical illustration of the horrors of
the lash would merely excite in a peaceable com-
munity an appetite for human suffering and
bananas.
This happened in Sydney, before the days
of the artillery reserves became numbered. One
of the crack batteries lacked its requisite strength
and to bring it up to its full complement authority
was given for recruiting. Some 20 odd men
presented themselves, were put through their pre-
liminary facings, and, seeming to promise well, a
“if . was set a Part for an examination as to
efficiency. When the evening arrived it was very
rainy, the examining officer had evidently lingered
long over the mess-dinner, and it was so dark
that the men’s features were undistinguish-
able. For some reason or other the recruits
failed to please the uniformed examiner, and
at the conclusion of an hour’s “test,” “His
Highness” swore roundly, told the candidates
they were a lob of fools and idiots, and
affirmed that even with the help of Heaven they
could not pass. He then left, but no sooner was
his back turned than a roar of laughter went up
from the men. It seemed that none of the recruits
had turned up, so, tb get the captain of the bat-
tery his efficiency-money, some of his best men
had combined to fool the examiner. That they
did not please him—men whose badges were of the
highest class—was put down to a disturbed liver.
Flowers fresh to thee I send,
Tokens of affection true,
Telling where my feelings blend
Like a magnet unto you.

BJ6 Macquarie-sfreet North, Sydney, Nov. 11,1891.
Wy Dear. Moorabinoa, —
Glancing foggily backwards at that already mildewed
excitement, the Melbourne Cup, which annually occurs a
day too late to allow The Bulletin to strike a fervent
attitude in several columns of rag-fair, padded out with
poitry ” about the wild woosh of horseflesh, and the pale
face of the eternal female whose Johnny is for ever at
Flemington staking his last lOU on a tricky bundle of
hoofs and hair—squinting solemnly backwards at the big
sartorial event, your Sappho seems as it she had passed ever
.so many hours in a coloured fashion-plate on that by-gone
Tuesday. Cup Day, like some of its devotees, was blue
and balmy, and as it has, I am told, been blue and balmy
for the last ten years, the thing must be getting mono-
tonous for the woman who looks best in a rain-proof
serge and an umbrella. The sunny weather hatched
summer frcckings, and the oblong lawn looked like a
chromotrope. Lady Hopetoun wore her favourite colour
(although it doesn’t become her best)—black, with Louis
scarf of turquoise blue crepe, tiny black topping-off with
blue forget-me-nots ; Lady Jersey encored that handsome
mixed-up gown of sap-green with moird pattern in black,
covered in black grenadine embroidered in a tiny pink
flower, bonnet en suite ; Lady Kintore wore ivory surah
trimmed with bands of lace insertion over prim-
rose, bonnet en suite ; Hopetoun’s lady cousins
wore bruise colours—that is, black and blue. The
other gowns were mostly soothers, but not at all cal-
culated to wring a sigh of hopeless envy from the bosom
of any woman who had a penny in the baby’s money-box
at home for the materials were mostly unassuming—
though it’s the cut that runs the total up. No colour
prevailed, though white and creamy tints had a big try
for first place, and white gamps bobbed up like toadstools
everywhere. Regarded as a shade-medium, a white
gamp is about as effective as a sheet of tissue-paper—but
they are “in” this summer and the swagger she must
chance sunstroke nowadays with her crownless gauze hat
and her unhned chiffon parasol. Long bodices, lace
basques, plain skirts, nothing new except a long, well-
fitting sleeveless jacket of fine black lace worn over a
turquoise-blue muslin, by a well-known Melbourne belle,
which was a charming resurrection (the jacket—not the
girl). Also, I thought I remarked a tendency on the part
of family groups to dress as foils to one another. Mamma
in heliotrope got Sissy up in delicate sea-green ; a girl in
iemon-colour was backed by her sister in pure white ; a
fawn frock gained life beside a mate in pale-blue—indeed,
charming contrasts chummed in so often that they got
to have quite a premeditated appearance.
A number of dresses were of flimsies over coloured
foundations—and one girl had blushingly chosen a
bilious tan for her slim-fit underslip, and the effect was
that of a sunburnt individual hurtling out of a bath in a
bed-curtain. I had a long, long look for the Cup belle,
but I sought vainly, for a great many of the girls were
pretty with a sort of suburban level of good looks, and
one pleased you almost as much as another; but it was
delightful to see that hardly one in twenty aided Nature
to any greater extent than powder-puff lengths. Of
course, there was the usual balance of shocking frooking;
the fattest female reckoned she was safe from criticism in
blue silk shot with crimson ; another wore a classically-
built black silk trimmed with gold with toga draperies
edged with Greek key pattern in gold ; a horrible old
woman with dyed hair and a patched-up “ as you will be”
visage, painted like a new house, trailed a bright-blue
train a yard long, and evidently regarded herself as one
of the ladies that make the lawn what it is, doncherknow;
and a red plush garment on a woman shaped like a cot-
tage loaf—one round atop of another—must have made
the sun wink its other eye.
The principal reason why I groaned audibly at Fleming,
ton on Oaks—yclept Ladies’—Day was the dozens o’
trussed-up doll-children torn round by stagey-minded
ma’s, who, I’ll bet, shake their offspring if they don’t call
them “dearest,” and train them like circus-dogs to coil
in graceful attitudes on hearth-rugs, and sich. The Aus-
tralian bantling doesn’t lend itself to the artistic. It
usually has a truculent expression and a bull voice, and it
won t act worth a split sixpence. It looks very very odd
to see Lord Fauntleroy rolling over in the dust and bel-
lowing because his gold-washed mother has run out of
jujubes, but while curls and sash-ribbon are cheap we
must just grin and bear it. There was a paucity of vice-
regal “ brow ” at the Oaks, only Lady Kintore lending a
subdued radiance to the Governor’s box with its faded
green chintz cushions and its eternal bunch of red roses.
The dresses were “ numerous and handsome,” but the one
that filled me with cold dread was a bright ghost-green
sparingly trimmed with black lace ; hat covered with
ghost-green feathers, and a ghost-green parasol which
gave the wearer’s face a phosphorescent ghastly light as
if from a corpse-candle. She looked as it the dead had
commenced to shriek and gibber in the streets of Mel-
bourne.
Steeple-chase day, on 7th instant, ended the V.R.C.
Cup meeting for 1891, and most people were content it
should be so. Four solid days of*crush and rags, of
horse and odds, are enough to satisfy the turfiest soul,
and there is a dreary monotony about the fourth break-
neck rush for a seat in the train ; the fourth hurried
lunch off uncooked farmyard patriarch—the fourth
jangle of “God Save the Queen” as the Governor rises
in the east. Women have a very genuine dread of jump-
ing-races, and yet they swarm into a racecourse if a
steeplechase is on the programme 1 and though they
loudly prognosticate, “Ooh! that one is sure to fall,” they
never turn their heads away even when the butterfly-
hued jockey is a rainbow heap on the green grass with his
horse falling atop of him. The vice-regal box had a
bevy of ladies. Lady Hopetoun resurrected her Cup
dress, with pink roses in her hat; Lady Kintore in etraw.
berry lined with a deeper colour and trimmed with bands
of strawberry velvet, ring bonnet of strawberry velvet
with cluster of lilies of the valley at the back ; Lady
Violet Willoughby wore her garden-party dress ; the rest
was also a twice-told tale. Melbourne’s friskiest spinster—
thoroughly reinstated in the social world because of the
big windfall lately dumped at her feet - looked innocence
itself in a simple gingham gown and a white straw hat;
the prettiest girl of last year’s Cup haunted the scene of
former victory in an artistic delaine and showed far more
than the clocks in her stockings when she hitched one
knee over the other in a ddgag6 way altogether
suggestive of cigar-boxes ; her papa’s red button-
holer suited him well in a Swinburnian sense -to my
thinking, Lothario should never wear a gardenia. Then
the touzled-headed woman, whose particular frisk with
a well-known doctor hasn’t been quite lived down,
dawdled along in aesthetic array. Somebody next points
out, dressed in gorgeous style, and simpering with half-
closed eyes at the grand-stand, “ the girl who ran off
with Mrs. Blank’s husband—of course, nobody knows her
now and following closely on her heels comes a fashion-
able and aggressive female, with false teeth and her eyes
lined round with black, who fires a red parasol at the
sun, and is described as “that poor Mrs. Dash, whose
husband ran away with the cash-box, you know, and left
her so miserable 1” Perhaps, after all, the grease-paint
and the fifty-guinea teeth are her way of wearing her
heart upon her sleeve.
The Cup this year is a very pawnable presentation-all
of solid silver. A partially-clothed young lady stands
erect on a sort of stump and holds out a wreath of oats
(typical of what the winner got up speed on) over a rather
ewe-necked steed and a mounted jockey, who gazes up-
wards with a rapt expression, but doesn’t attempt to
grab. It is a beautiful ornament, and looks as if it would
yield just the right number of ounces to the pound when
it reaches the inevitable melting-pot.
Melbourne vice-regal Cup ball wasn’t graced by Lady
Hopetoun, who left Lady J. and Lady K. to do the affable
business, but all the same it was a great success, the
diamonds of a snow-fair Sydneyite making the company
wink—as also did the clever frescoes of the women from
the Sydney side of the Murray, who were nearly all care-
fully-shaded-off-towards-the-jawbone beauties with an
emergency slab of Chinese vegetable rouge in their
pockets. On 6th instant the gubernatorial fete chant-
petre came off and a lovely day ensured all acceptances
and no scratchings, though the sun was too ardent for
some of the makes-up present, which got fearsomely
blotchy as the artist porspir&l. The dressing was mostly
da-capo —all of it had been seen at the» races. Lady
Hopetoun wore black and turquoise, blue bonnet en suite.
Lady Jersey—a favourite in Melbourne—in a mottled-grey
brocade like a frock made out of zinc, withdrew from
the gay and festive scene to catch the homeward express;
Lady Kintore encored herOupfrooking; Lady Willoughby
in shot terra-cotta with black grenadine, black hat with
pink roses. Comprehensive stacks of human fuel were
arranged in big marquees on the lawn, and a band backed
up the talk nicely, as usual—but the immovable police-
men set here and there about the grounds seemed to
imply that the company individually wasn’t the Hope-
touns’ dearest friend, and that it was sadly necessary for
somebody to keep an eagle eye on the cutlery.
Lady Hopetoun, it seems, goes out of her way to snub
the glittering society folk of “ Melbawne,” who, in their
aggressive turn, love to make unkind sotto voce remarks
when they think she is close enough to hear. The first
objection to the vice-regal mavourneen is, that she per-
sists in regarding the large white-vested Toorakian, who
made his little heap at vending rags wholesale, in the same
light as she does the little retail draper round the corner,
who does his own cheating—and treats them both in
exactly the same way. Now, in the bright lexicon of
Australian society the big softgoodsman who wags his
coat-tails into a building as big as a bank, with marble
steps and plate-glass doors, at ten a.m. daily, is a social
Somebody, whose wife and girls talk on the topmost note,
and whose vociferous disgust at “ trade” is almost whim-
sical under the circumstances, although most people
seem to swallow it and say, in a hushed voice, “how
dreadfully proud the Linsey-Woolseys are 1” Therefore,
the “first lady” in any Australian state must not strip
off the glamour which a carriage-and-pair and a dazzling
livery give to the man who really is only a trader, and
reveal the “ utter social impossibility ” of the tall-hatted
pomposity and his Paris-frooked family to a community
which has hitherto blindly reverenced him as the embodi-
ment of the inner circle. The vice-regal colleen calmly
does so—and marks the social value of her slighting
“how de do?” by sometimes even shaking hands with the
most ordinary shopman who ministers to her needs over
the counter. Which is the great reason why Lady
Hopetoun is spared the gushing affection of her temporary
subjects.
Melbourne sassiety is greatly exercised to know how
the married heroine of the aide-de-(s)camp will be received
by the local social world on her approaching return ? Let
me be a fair weather prophetess and predict open arms.
Why not? Doth not the divorcee cavort in the marble
halls of gubernatorial residence? and the frisky female,
doth she not kiss the staid vice-regal woman on both
cheeks ? It isn’t as if everybody didn’t know all about it.
There is a very promising Australian scandal about the
lady and her hopeless adorer who had a daring soul and a
fertile mind. It was at a ball held at a Town-hall, and
strolling down a corrider, “ Will you come in here ? ” into
So-and-So’s nicely-furnished office? Scarcely had the
door closed when a knock came, and he pretended that
“if we are discovered we are lost! ” rushed to the door and
locked it, but unfortunately the applicant was the man
who occupied the office, and, as it wasn’t opened to him
after repeated battering, he retired in disgust leaving a
menial to see who came out. When they did emerge the
news was all over the ball-room like wild-fire. Behind
the locked door Gussy had only proposed and been
rejected, but, of course, nobody wanted to think so, and
the lady further enhanced the situation by fainting away.
Whether she will marry him or not is the question which
agitates the scandal-mongers who shake their withered
heads over the lesson it is to girls to keep in the beaten-
track of verandah-lounges and conservatory chairs!
Lady Jersey certainly isn’t the first possibility in the
shape of a good dresser, but she alone, of all the vice-regal
women who adorned Melbourne Cup week, was the only
one who did not sartorially repeat herself. All the rest
did—horribly, but M. E. Jersey, who, as a litterateuse,
might be excused from this sort of thing, changed her
plumage every day. As a vice-regal clothes-peg, Lady J.
can now go top.

The Bulletin regrets to hear that the
best Melbourne paper has lost its best writer.
H. J. Johnstone, the distinguished Vic-
torian artist, pupil of Louis Buvelot, is in London
and far on the road to fame.
Says Dr. Mortimer Granville, the cham-
pion of alcohol: “Man is a brewing as well as a
cooking animal, and he will sink to barbarism in
proportion as he ceases to brew and to cook.”
Anyone who wants evidence of this should dine at
a restaurant where they sell no liquor.
Ye unbelieving Thomases read this ! Mrs.
Therle, 62 Bathurst-street, hotel proprietress
writes Many thanks for the bottles of Mar-
shall’s Tonic Nervine sent as requested. I am
taking it regularly and deem it of incomparable
value as compared with any tonic I have hitherto
used. I consider it a most excellent restorative,
and find that my appetite has greatly improved
since taking it, as well as the tone of my health
generally.
The big Anglo-Australian Co.’s suspension
last week certainly “ did not come as a surprise”
for all the town of Sydney knew what the daily
papers were ignorant of (one of the Anglo directors,
curiously enough, is or was a very big shareholder
m the Sydney D.T., and another is the ex-editor
of the S. M. Herald) —viz., that weeks ago the
company stopped paying for several hours. The
dailies promptly showed up all the comparatively
small failures, but never a hint did they give of
the biggest one of all.
“Now, what are your witnesses prepared
to swear?” asked Mr. daggers, the criminal
lawyer itl “Great Expectations,” to one of his scaly
clients. *‘ Well,” replied the latter, “in a general
sort of way, almost anything.” This answer
seems to have peculiar application to some police-
men’s evidence, and after the reversal of Stipen-
diary Lee’s decision in the case of Petty-Officer
Caseley, of the Mildura, it will be decidedly in-
teresting to observe how the authorities will deal
with the constables who seemingly combined to
swear away a sober, respectable man’s liberty and
character.
As a sequel to the Queensland Parlia-
mentary vote of £2OOO for the widow and family
of thelate JackMacrossan, Mr. Buckland, M.L. A.
has successfully moved for a similar State gratuity
to the widow and family of thelate Henry Jordan,
a deceased statesman chiefly remarkable for his
undying faith in wholesale immigration, for his
devout Wesleyanism, for his interest in land
legislation, and his party fidelity to all Queen-
street Governments. He was so generally credited
with having no personal vice whatever that he
never was at all popular. In the early days of
Moreton Bay, the unemployed new-chums sent
out from the Queensland Immigration office in
London, used to parade Queen-street howling in
chorus—“ We’ve got no work to do-00-ooh !” They
were nicknamed “Jordan’s Lambs.”
Thus Schey, M.P., secretary to the inter-
provincial railway convention ;
The service in N.S.W. is in a most deplorable condition
—honeycombed, infested, with pimps, sneaks and spies.
This abominable system of espionage has resulted in most
disgusting exhibitions of faithlessness, and it is disgrace-
ful that the country allows the expenditure of a large sum
of money annually in perpetuating the dastardly practice.
In Queensland, affairs are much worse. The Commis-
sioners have used every endeavour to break up the local
associations. Officers of the department have even
resorted to the illegal device of opening the employes’
letters, with the object of ascertaining whether the latter
were in sympathy with the recent strike.
It was further stated by the Queensland delegate
that the Queensland Coniinissi oners’ repressive
measures, combined with the espionage system,
had reduced the local association’s membership
from 700 to 350.
The head of the house of Fairfax, known
in the S.M. Herald as “ Mister James,” is back,
and has been given by his devoted employes,
duly assembled in the Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall,
the usual return welcome, consisting chiefly of
psalms, prayer, and lemonade. He was musically
denominated “a Jolly Good Fellow,” which is
somewhat surprising, for though the Fairfaxes
(and their papers) are all “good,” not to say
goody-goody, none of them have been known to
be singularly jolly up to date. Mr. Sam. Cook,
business manager, “took occasion,” as usual, to
refer to the Herald, Mail and Echo as “great
papers,’’—which they certainly are in the sense of
containing a thundering lot of paper and
remarked that the Herald’s new double-web
machines “produced newspapers at the rate of
24,000 an hour.” For how many hours a day,
Samuel ?
