SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Criterion “School.”
Theatre Royal “Robbery Under Arms.”
Garrick “Run Wild.”
“Run Wild,” at Sydney Garrick, is the
sad tale of a long female’s repentance, and the
regeneration of an oily cad. The female is
Parker’s wife ; she moves in high society, and has
an eye like the front lamp of a locomotive, and a
fierce underjaw that sticks out after the manner of
a bull-dog’s head ; and the oily one is Parker’s
son. Parker himself is a self-made man, and he
looks dreadfully like the sort of man that a man
that looks like him would be likely to make. He
is honourable, cheerful, and stumpy, and his
clothes are a bad misfit. His wife repudiates him,
principally by reason of his stumpiness and his
misfit; and his son disguises their relationship by
passing his parent off as a distant acquaintance
named Jones. His brother-in-law, a haughty
baronet with an all-seeing eye, casts him off as he
would cast off a worm, and nobody cares for the
lonesome Parker except his daughter and her dog.
He is a sad, broken-spirited millionaire, till his
cotton-mill burns down with no insurance on it,
and then there is a change. His wife returns
with a tear shining in her fierce eye, and flings
herself up against him and tells him that she will
never leave him any more. Then the son looms in
the doorway weeping silently into his hat; he also
has come back to repent, and he also leans up
against Parker, and in a contrite spirit tells him
that he will see him through. The daughter and
the dog also undertake to see Parker through.
The elongated and noble youth who is engaged to
the daughter guarantees to stick to him, and the
silent man who is going to marry Parker’s niece
intimates, by a glare and a nod, that he will prop
up Parker to the last gasp. Even the son’s rowdy
acquaintances seem inclined to rally round Parker
at this crisis, and, last of all, the baronet enters
with a great resolution on his face, determined to
stand or fall by Parker. Then it transpires that
Parker is all right ; he had sold the mill two weeks
previously, and, consequently, he isn’t ruined
after all. Presumably the man he sold it to is
mined instead, but that man isn’t in the bill, so
nobody seems to care about him. The story ends
amid general rejoicing, and only The Bulletin
man sheds a single tear over the fate of that lost,
unnoticed, nameless, friendless individual who
bought Parker’s mill and was burnt out.
# * *
“ Run Wild ” is a good play all the same.
Jennie Lee, as Parker’s daughter, is a girl who is
calculated to gladden any man’s heart, and with
such a maiden to cheer his declining years the
old man’s lamentations over the lost wife with the
combustible eye are badly misplaced. Frank
Cates is another gaudy success as Jennie’s future
husband, an individual who is too long to be an
inhabitant of this short earth, and too excited to
be an angel. A certain H. Diver is an absolute
genius as an easy-going person with his jewellery
in pawn ; E. P. Carey does old Parker with much
success, and Richard Stewart does young Parker
with comparatively little. When he comes back
repentant and wailing into his hat he is, in fact,
within an ace of utter failure. The other bright
features of the show are Mr. Shine, Miss Con-
stance Deorwyn, and the dog. This animal badly
wants to eat Shine in the second act, and unless
that gentleman learns to howl less and run a good
deal more, some portion of his anatomy will be
butchered one of these nights to make a dog’s
holiday at the Garrick.
** * *
That stupendous work of art, Sells’ Circus,
opens at Moore Park, Sydney, on Thursday next
(November 19). According to public rumour, it
is a huge show, containing all the animals ever
seen or heard of, and a lot more that have been
lying low, at great expense, ever since the Crea-
tion, in order to make their first appearance on
this auspicious occasion. Also, it contains a lot
more animals that have been newly made by
special arrangement with Providence. This latter
is a deserving institution, and Sells’ Circus has
always made it a point to back up Providence,
and lend it every encouragement. It was origin-
ally proposed to open this week, but a gentleman,
whose card describes him as “ Charles Stow,
Editor,” informs us of the change of plan. We
haven’t seen Mr. Stow, and we don’t actually
know what a gentleman who edits a circus is like.
But we once knew a man who was sub-editor to a
learned pig, and another who was dramatic critic
to a hippopotamus, and they were both exceed-
ingly agreeable individuals. Also, we have been
introduced to a person who acted as poet to a
performing serpent, and he was about the nicest
man we ever knew.
** * *
“ School” is doing good business at Syd-
ney Criterion. There are whole stacks of girls in
this comedy—slender, poetic girls with dreamy
faces ; plump, jovial girls whose hair won’t keep
straight; sad, soulful girls who look as if they
had a tendency to be religious and die young;
girls with dark hair and affectionate dark eyes ;
other girls with golden hair and blue eyes; more
girls with all kinds of hair and brown eyes; oceans
of girls with all sorts and descriptions of eyes;
and a story with so many girls in it can’t by any
possibility be a failure. But unluckily there are
only two beauteous lovers lurking in the garden,
and they are a very small allowance compared to
such a tremendous lot of girls. It is very dark, and
damp, and wormy in that garden, and there amid
the dew and the caterpillars, and the slimy insects
that crawl on their stomachs by reason of not
having any legs, the lovers lie waiting on the off-
chance of seeing some curly brown head at the
window or hearing a soft titter through a key-
hole. And here the story isn’t true to life, and
the author doesn’t know what he is talking about.
The present writer also lurked outside a girl’s
school once in the hope of seeing a little, glossy,
golden wig at the casement, and he can tell by
experience exactly what the consequences of such
a proceeding are. The window opened on
that occasion; in the faint, romantic moon-
light there appeared the shimmer of a
snow-white robe, and a white neck, and
a rounded arm that was too beautiful
for this dull, material earth. Then a musical
voice said something in an undertone, and the
writer hastily shook the spiders and the green
bugs out of his flute, and prepared to strike up a
serenade. Next moment he was almost blinded
by a half-eaten pie, which fetched him in the left
eye, and he was also shot by a ginger-beer bottle.
Tiie angels were having a surreptitious feed in the
bedroom—a feed of coarse and common pork-pie,
and they had nearly blown his brains out with
the fragments. There is mighty little romance in
a girls’ academy after all, and a crippled person,
who went home that night with a broken flute
and the remains of a bottle embedded in his
system, hereby lifts up his voice and says that
Robertson is a delusion.
* * *
“ School ” is an interesting comedy, how-
ever, because the midnight feed element is kept
in the background, and no one explains that the
girl with the dreamy eyes is only dreaming of
tarts and unwholesome buns. Messrs. Titheradge,
Gordon, Lyndal, and Boucicault keep up the
heavy end of the show. The latter individual
makes a glittering success as that ancient ruin,
Parintosh, he of the wig, and the old shop teeth,
and the venerable corns, and the immoral glass
eye, and the unutterable togs. Mr. Lawrence
does Krux, the pallid sneak whose ear, like that
of the Sydney Social Purity Society, has been
enlarged with much listening at keyholes, and
whose moist and filmy optic, also like that
of Sydney Purity Society, is worn out through
being fixed for many years at the cracks of doors,
and exposed to the draught from the other side.
Also, Misses Spong, Romer, and Pattie Browne,
make up the balance of the show, and as there is
no tail to the Criterion company they are all that
any reasonable white man could desire.
** * *
“The Dancing Girl” has been dancing
this week on her last legs at Her Majesty’s (Syd-
ney), No one seems to regret her much, and her
MADAME MELBA.
funeral promises to be unobtrusive. The min-
strelsy business is prospering at the School of
Arts, and the Cycloraraa continues to grind up
the old Battle of Gettysburg on the old grindstone
of history with remorseless regularity. It makes
us feel sorry when we think of that painted
encounter and the man who won it. It was about
the biggest battle of the long war. and it broke
the back of the Southern Confederacy; and
yet Meade is almost forgotten because he
didn’t know how to advertise himself when the
war was over. If the famous Smythe had got
hold of him immediately after Gettysburg, and
fixed him for a course of lectures, he would have
made a pile.
** * *
“Robbery Under Arms” is doing good
business at Sydney Royal to the last. Even if
the play were eighteen times worse than it is,
Dampier and the scenery would pull it through.
In fact, Dampier, when at his best, could pull
anything through, and if we had a ponderous
bullock-dray stuck in a quicksand we would put
our faith in Alfred’s haulage capacity sooner than
in a team of 16 horses. Next Saturday the
“ Corsair ” goes up with a strong company. He
is a melodious bass bandit in B flat or thereabouts,
and judging by our personal experience of bandits
we presume that he opens a bank in his ensan-
guined lair, and drags his shrieking captives there
in chains with their long, golden hair hanging
down their backs, and then, while they pray for
death as a merciful release, he brutally compels
them to put their money on fixeddeposit for three
years at 10 per cent.
** * *
Bondi Aquarium has a bill a yard long,
and what with Hungarian bell-ringers, champion
lady skaters, Egyptian jugglers, a “ suspended
lady ” —which doesn’t mean a hanged lady—min-
strel shows, concerts, brass bands, zither players,
a suicidal individual who walks on a wire, a huge
concert, a farce, caves, seals, fish, and a lot of
other things, it makes a huge shillingsworth.
Manager Wyburd boasts that 16,000 people
haunted his show on the bald-headed Heir-
Apparent’s birthday. From this we gather that
Tummy’s birthday is a lot more useful to Wyburd
than it is to Tummy himself.
* * #
“The Jonquille ” three-nights season at
the Adelaide Royal was a dismal failure. As
already stated, the revival was to be for the benefit
of the young author, Guy Boothby, who was in
hopes of a tidy cheque to cover portion of his
expenses to England, but, alas, it was not to be,
and with Boothby all is lost save honour and the
MS. of “The Jonquille,” which may yet turn up
a trump card. At present, however, the drama-
tist has given up all hope of going to England, and
rumour has it that he intends retiring in a satur-
nine frame of mind to a northern station, where
he will commune gloomily with Nature. Some of
his critics think this will be rough on Nature, but
The Bulletin feels sure that institution will pull
through. Possibly, in the solemn bush, Mr.
Boothby may begin to write dramas of the George
Darrell type, but we trust not. The theatre has
been closed for a week. Next issue we shall refer
to the production of “Human Nature,” by the
Gerald-Duff company, and either explain to our
own satisfaction why it failed, Or prove conclu-
sively that we foresaw its success from the
beginning, and that we gave Gerald his first start
in life, and frequently patted him on the head
when he was a boy.
** * *
Cup week has given big houses to Mel-
bourne shows—mostly to the Princess and Alex-
andra. That venerable idiot, “ The Old Guard,”
is well supported by the public and a quantity of
assorted female legs, likewise Wm. Elton a,nd
Eraisette. This astounding hunk of entertaining
nothingness seems to draw quite as well as the
braininess of Gilbert and Sullivan, a fact generally
attributable to the shapely extremities of the
ballet. There is no sign of a change at the big
house just yet.
«« * «
“ A Night Off ” at Melbourne Bijou
becomes less pleasing on continued acquaintance.
It does not approach within shooting distance of
the previous production at the Princess. Anson
is the only one of the original Australian cast,
and even he introduces a buffoonery that is rather
painful. There is, of course, the prejudicial
influence of an earlier experience of the play, but
even allowing for that, the present cast is not
equal to the old one. Considering all things,
however, the show is feeling very well, thank you,
although Miss Bronton is not too satisfactory as
the wife of the professorial crank man.
** * *
At the Opera House, that alleged comic
opera, “The Corsair,” finished Tuesday last, and
as a consequence Melbourne has decided to hold
a special week of thanksgiving. Brigand Stanley
started to tell the people how he found a man
named Livingstone on Wednesday night, and
continues thereon until Saturday. There is
nothing fixed for next Monday, and on that date
“Corsair” or the blood-curdling “Evangeline”
may be revived. And it will take a lot to revive
either anyway.
** * *
The Siege of Paris Cyclorama, in Bourke-
street, has done better than any other show since
last time of writing. Manager Forster is still
lecturing on that incident of European hate and
idiocy. The halls do well as usual, and the
Gaiety niggers open their heads quite as much as
they used to do.
** * *
“Faust” has drawn immense houses to
the barnlike Alexandra, on Cuttica nights espe-
cially. The lady is a magnificent Margherita,
but her hubby makes a very poor Faust. Melossi,
as Valentine, puts in some fine work, and his
singing in the duel and death scenes provides
holier amusement than a dog-fight in which both
dogs are chawed into fragments. The Mephisto.
of Traviaglini is a good-voiced devil who can’t act,
and Signorina Mattioli’s Siebel is a refreshing
little fellow in cream tights. “Sonnambula,”
with Cuttica as Amina, held the fort on Monday
and Tuesday, and “Trovatore” was put on
Wednesday. To-night (Thursday) “Lucia” will
be revived, and to-morrow the tenor, Yilalta,
benefits with the successful “Faust.” Madame
finishes the Melbourne season with a big concert
at the Town Hall on Saturday, and the Alexandra
will probably be occupied after that by the ever-
green Dam pier.
** * *
“ Lights o’ London ” died a sudden death
on Wednesday, and George Rignold puffed and
floundered in the tank, and showed people he
was becoming elderly for the last time. The
next show at this house will be “ Rob Roy,” with
Walter Bentley in the name part. Bentley, up to
date, at any rate, does not look like success, for
the crowd supporting him is, for the purposes of
Scotch drama, very haggard. Eloise Juno will be
the Helen Macgregor, and the Bailie has been
given to Cathcart, who, though a sterling actor,
was not designed by Heaven for a dialect part.
Juno was the best Helen in Australia at one
time, and so a saving clause is inserted in her
favour.
At Poverty Point.
Lucia Harwood temporarily filled Fanny Brough’s
place in “A Sailor’s Knot,” at Drury Lane, and with con-
siderable success. It is wonderful what a little Australian
experience generally does for actors and actresses. Per-
haps it is that they hear “ home truths” more frequently
in Australia than they do in England.
Apropos to the recent scenes in the Opera House, Paris,
on the occasion of the production of “ Lohengrin,” it is
recorded that when “ Othello” was performed by a Lon-
don dramatic company at the Theatre de la Porte St.
Martin, in 1822, some interested people spread the report
that Shakespeare was one of Wellington's aides-de-camp
at Waterloo. This was enough for the shop-boys of the
Faubourg Saint Denis—they stormed the theatre.
Haddon Chambers is in request; for, in addition to the
play he is writing for Thorne, he has been commissioned
by Mrs. John Wood to construct one for her. It is very
doubtful, however, whether Haddon’s style will suit a
Court audience. The Court is essentially a comedy
house, and Haddon’s humour is of the obscure order. It
took weeks to work up the comedy business in “ The
Idler,” and even at its best it is the weakest part of the
play.
Harold Frederic has had to resign membership of the
Savage in consequence of an allegation that he had made
certain rash assertions about the wife of a fellow member.
He is a loss to the club, but his offence appears to have
been too gross to be overlooked. There are rumours i,n
the air of libel-actions and all kinds of things in connec-
' tion with this affair. “Doyvid” Anderson, the inventor
and proprietor of the school of journalists is one of those
who are to be shot at.
George Coppin has an opportunity of immortalising
himself. Let him import, if he can secure them, Daly’s
company. I promise you, writes our correspondent, that
you have never had such a treat in Australia, and I do not
doubt that the speculation would be a great success. Ada
Rehan alone is worth going ten thousand miles to see. A
more consummate artist never walked the boards. Her
latest London hit has been in “ The Last Word.” Never
surely was such uproarious applause heard in any theatre
as saluted her performance in the second act, when the
house fairly went wild. Ellen Terry never had such an
ovation at the Lyceum, and that is saying a good deal.
It was thought when he left for Australia that Laurence
Cautley’s eyes would fetch ’em. The Savage Club
wonders if the “ widdy ” is gilded.
George Musgrove is living at Weybridge. Nellie Stewart
is not to be the Blue-Eyed Susan in the new comic sing-
song at the Shaftesbury. The part has been offered to
the giddy Florence St. John, who will probably accept it.
M. Marius is to stage-manage. What are the odds
against a reconciliation and another marriage ?
“ My Sweetheart ” is drawing good paying houses at
London Vaudeville, and yours merrily, Jno. R. Rogers,
and Minnie are like a pair of turtle doves. The quarrel
and separation yarn was only one of J.R.’s advertising
dodges.
L. S. Mapleson has written an account of Adelina Patti’s
last tour in Mexico. The Diva received no less than
£34,000 for 30 performances. Tamagno, the tenor, was
paid £4OO a night. The result of the Mexican visit was
that the National Mont de Pibt6 was besieged for weeks
by members of impecunious but proud families, who pre-
ferred to pledge all belonging to them rather than it
should appear that they were unable to afford the
exorbitant prices charged for seats.
Williamson left the London Adelphi because he
wouldn’t act the Shaughraun without Boucicault’s leave.
Bentley is entertained by Hopetoun—Bernhardt wasn’t.
Madame Melba begun by playing the violin, harp, and
organ, and had no idea she was a vocalist while playing
the harmonium in church.
Alderman Tom Moubray, Melb., left £188,900, and his
sister, widow of Panorama Rainer, gets a little, we are
glad to hear.
On the last night of a burlesque, which recently had a
big run in the English provinces, the actors and actresses
tore up as much as they could of their make-up, and
flung them to the audience, as souvenirs. It wouldn’t do
for the average burlesque girl to throw away tew mutch.
Mary Anderson regularly puts flowers on Mrs. Siddons’
grave—which looks like a hankering after the theatrical
fleshpots. It also looks like a characteristically modest
stage advt.—“ Me and Missis Siddons.”
R. S. Smythe is to be the next Archbishop of Canter-
bury.
Miss Mary Kingsley leaves Australia tor England per
Orizaba on 14th inst.
At latest advices—per cable—the British public had
begun to throw bricks at our late “ distinguished visitor,”
poor, dull, old Johnny Toole.
Walter Bentley (the coming Rob Roy) has a brother of
the representative Tory order, who howls for Imperial
federation, takes an active interest in the Primrose
League, and threatens to stand for one of the London
boroughs at next general elections. Walter himself is
understood to be a bit of a Radical.
J. C. Williamson paid Nellie Farren a graceful parting
compliment. Wybert Reeve, inspired by Jimmy, sent
Treasurer Whittington and assistants down to the Poly-
nesien at Port Adelaide to lavishly decorate Nellie’s state
cabin with flowers and make the place look like a small
Paradise. The Farren was ashore doing the Block, and
consequently knew nothing about it.
Meston’s programme for the forthcoming tour of
Europe and America with a troupe of Queensland niggers
includes eleven different kinds of corroboree. Meston,
solus, once gave the Johnsonian Club, in Brisbane, a
corroboree, dressed up in a loin-wrapper and burnt-cork,
varnish, with red-and-white raddle facings, and a door-
mat wig. Attention, please, R. S. Smythe.

Frederick Hobson. Ergo, Nellie Farren Hobson’
choice ?

POLITICAL POINTS
Let the Single-taxers leave generalities
alone and give ns calculations based on the official
statisticians’ figures.
By virtue of the system of plural voting
six members of the present N.S.W. Ministry have
votes in East Sydney.
In three years Parkes has bestowed upon
political supporters the largo sum of <£25,600 in
commissioners’ fees !
In his early career as a legislator, Parkes
made several attempts to abolish the death-
penalty, but could never secure a majority.
“ The trouble is this : not that I am setting
Protection above Federation, but that I will not
set Freetrade above Federation.” — Barton.
East Sydney, the citadel of Freetrade, has
returned some distinguished Protectionists to the
Assembly. Parkes, Martin, Buchanan, Barton.
Solomon’s motion in S.A. Assembly for
the abolition of capital punishment was rejected
by 23 to 12. This vote represents a satisfactory
ad vance.
W. E. Abbott and J. F. Burns might have
been shoved into the Nominee Council (N.S.W.)
if Parkes had remained in office a few months
longer.
A. G. Taylor would not, on principle,
oppose Toby Barton for East Sydney. His views
were not the same in 1884, when he helped Bur-
dekin to defeat G. H. Reid.
The Imperial Institute authorities are very
much disgusted with Sir Henry Parkes for not
redeeming Sir Patrick Jennings’ promise of a
liberal contribution from N.S.W.
The Dibbs Ministry have agreed to talk
but ten minutes on a question. Slattery and
Copeland might as well be gagged. It takes these
politicians an hour to work off an idea.
Mr. Eli Moremon (Memo, for printer: This
name to be spelt very carefully. —En. B.) proposed
candidate Grantham at East Sydney hustings.
Moremon ! Belongs to the Parkes party, you bet.
Black Wednesday is ahead in New South
Wales. The absolute success of the Democratic
party will be signalised by the application of the
axe to the root of the monstrously overgrown Civil
f-g.r vice.
C. C. Kingston, Q.C. (S.A.): —“I look
forward to the time—and I hope it is not far
distant—when no true unionist will work with a
blackleg, just the same as no master would employ
a person of that class.”
Parkes approved of £11,500 being spent
upon a statue of Governor Phillip, and yet ho
grudgingly placed £2oooon the N.S.W. Estimates
(on condition of £2OOO being subscribed) towards
a statue of John Dunmore Lang !
Is it essential that a Cabinet Minister
should submit the Commonwealth Bill to the
N.S.W. Assembly? It is not a Government
measure, but a mere draft of a constitution. Why
not allow’ Parkes to run his fad to the death ?
The Tasmanian Ministry got so savage
under the criticisms of the press that they came
down with a Defamation Bill which punished a
person with two years’ imprisonment who de-
famed a member of Parliament ! It was rejected
by the Council.
Premier Dibbs: “There are only two
things that can be produced in N.S.W. as cheaply
as they can bo imported. These are, mutton and
beef, and, without protection, the whole country
wmuld soon be devoted to the production of these
two things.”
D. Melville, M.L.C., Vic. (on plural
votes): “ Yet it is pretended that these thieves —
these land-jobbers who rob no one man but every
man—are more valuable and more reliable than
industrious workmen who bring up their families
respectably and die poor.”
The Sydney D.T., after printing 20 leaders
or more against the election of Toby Barton for
East Sydney, jumped Jim Crow on election day
and called upon the Freetraders to vote for the
Protectionist Minister. The paper has now boomed
in turn almost every politician in the province.
It must not be forgotten that, notwith-
standing the fact that all their theoretical curses
are properly directed against the man who buys
State land cheap and makes profit out of the un-
earned increment, the N.S.W. Single-tax League
proposed to exempt the free-selectors from the tax.
Parkes has not yet closed his political
diary. He intends to see the Dibbs Ministry take
their seats on the Treasury benches. Will he dare
to plunge the Assembly into confusion on the
fiscal question ? Or is he merely going to the
House to watch the course of John Haynes and
George Reid ?
The exact population of Bananaland is
393,718 persons—223,779 males and 169,939 females.
During the last five years the males have increased
17.56 per cent, and the females 28.25 per cent ;
during the decade —males, 78.56 per cent, females'
92.67 per cent. Woman is still wanted by the
fervent Banana man.
Member Hancock thinks South Australia
could get cheaper money in other countries than
Great Britain. The nagging of the London sharks
and papers may yet force an appeal to the money-
dealers of Paris, Berlin, and New York. And
why not ? The money-market is slowly, but
surely, shifting from London.
The N.S.W. Freetraders are now as hard-
up for a leader as ever the Protectionists were. If
Parkes retires will G. H. Reid, the deadly
opponent of Federation at any price, upon whom,
in that respect, has fallen the mantle of old Jack
Robertson, lead the party of Frectrade and Federa-
tion ? He is the Freetraders’ most popular man.
The Dobsons have been leading figures in
Tasmanian politics for many years. Chief Justice
Dobson is the head of the family: he was
Attorney-General in several administrations.
Henry Dobson, a wealthy attorney, has the
reversion of the Premiership in his hands. Alfred
Dobson has been successively Attorney-General,
Speaker, and Solicitor-General.
This is the very latest in the way of loyal
Tory corruption. Under the Macdonald reyime
in Canada nearly every official of any standing
had a mistress, who was registered as a civil
servant at a good screw. The nation paid for
everything ; if a public functionary even stood
oysters and stout to a ballet-girl, he charged it to

The Bulletin no more understands why
the N.S. Wales Labour-party should make a
special dead-set against Barton at East Syd-
ney than it understands why it opposed Traill
for South Sydney,- or why Dibbs excluded
from his Government both Traill and O'Sullivan
in favour of a nonenity like Postmaster-General
Kidd, who is apparently a mere sop to Orangeisin,
just as Parkes’ Postmaster-General was a top to
the opposite party.
Crick, M.P. (N.S. W,), after his experience
with the late Premier, has forsworn all complicity
with political appointments, especially those of
an Exhibition nature. “Look hero!” he ex-
claimed, the moment that a request was broaohed
to him the other day in King-street, “I wouldn’t
try to get a Civil Service appointment in Aus-
tralia, Banjcewangie, or in Boorioboola-crha, not
even for my own brother, and, thank God, I
haven’t got one.”
The (Foreign) Agreements Validating Act
(N.S.W.), which enables the enforcement of
labour-contracts made abroad, after 10 years’
operation is sought to bo repealed by the Demo-
crats. It had a stormy passage through the
Assembly. The third reading was carried by the
Speaker’s vote. Sir John Robertson moved that
the bill do now pass ; but the votes being again
equal, the Speaker voted with Ihe uoes ; and it
was only after some delay and a long debate that
the measure got through.
Premier Dibbs has informed a Wagga
audience that “the appointment of members to the
Legislative Council is one of the prerogatives that
can be exercised by the Governor, if ho chooses,
without the advice of his Ministers.” That may
be technically constitutional law, but the first
Australian Governor who tried the experiment of
swamping the Council with his personal nominees
would soon find himself without Ministerial
Advisers —and out of a billet.
A Bill has been introduced into N.S.W.
Legislature to legitimatise, on the marriage of the
parents, children born out of wedlock. This has
long been the law in Scotland, and it is difficult
to understand how any country professing to be
liberal in politics and Christian in religion could
be otherwise. It is barbarous and illogical to
continue to punish innocent children for the
laches of their parents, and the only fault with the
measure under notice is that it does not go far
enough.
The three-legged provisional proposals * f
Premier Griffith having been defeated on practical
formulation by 33 to 28 votes, the Queensland
Northern and Central members will agitate for
territorial separation and nothing else. The
Coalition Government is tired of the present bal-
ance-step-withont-gaining-ground Parliament and
will probably dissolve. Griffith and Co. are
quietly consolidating the voting chances, and
MTlwraith is prepared (on compulsion) to retire
gracefully.
Under Victorian Minister Outtrim’s new
Mines Bill, a most drastically radical measure,
the present facilities afforded to miners for gold
and silver-mining upon private property and upon
Crown-leased or Crown-licensed lands will bo
extended to mining for coal, tin, antimony, and
other minerals. In fact, the Bill seeks to estab-
hsh the Crown right to all minerals in or upon the
land, to give the Crown power to resume or enter
upon any mineral land, or to grant permission to
private persons to do so.
r In formally opening the new Melbourne
Trades-hall last week, Chairman Winter wept
over the unavoidable absence of Gov. Hopetoun,
thus .indicating fin underlying tendency to idol-
worship which should have no place in Labour's
home-paddock concerns. If a trades body can’t
shoot out a new, live claw without yearning for
the presence and patronage of a foreign nominee,
wlk se very place in an alleged democracy is the
symbol of the restriction of that democracy’s free-
dom, it is not worthy of its mission.
Speaking at East Sydney, Attorney-General
Barton said that Pulsford’s excuse when he re-
signed theN.S. W. Freetradesecretaryship was that
“tlu; creature Protection ” was dead. “Whereas,”
said Barton, “it seemed that it was only the
N.S.W. Frectrade exchequer which was deceased,
and if Protection was really dead, how came it
that they now found a live Protectionist Ministry
in office?” “Pooh!” said a voice in the crowd,
“that is only a resurrection!” “Resurrection,*’
replied Barton, “is the beginning of immortality.’
W. S. Target!, once member for Hartley
(N.S.W.), and James Coombe, were asked by the
Tasmanian Assembly to explain their conduct at a
public meeting. The answers were curt and
cutting; and the House at once instructed the
Attorney-General to amend the law so as to reach
such vulgar critics in the future. Targett de-
nounced the conduct of the Ministry as “a
disgrace to a civilised community,” while Coombe
wrote to the House —“One would think from the
eagerness of the present Government to pass syn-
dicate bills they were affected by the almighty
dollar in the way of tips.” The'VJ.ended House
was surprised to discover that it could not punish
for contempt committed outside its walls.
“ It is true we (be., S.A.) have a Lcgisla
tive Chamber representing property, and we have
a Governor appointed by the Queen. The first is
an anachronism that must soon be corrected. The
second, a relic introduced for spectacular purposes,
which will continue until we begin to think. The
second chamber, as at present elected and consti-
tuted, is under sen'cnco of death without hope of
reprieve. It may come to a sudden end, or it may
linger out a little longer, but it is so absolutely
contrary to the spirit of the age that it would not
surprise us if it were lynched The
Governor, like the Queen, reigns, but does not
govern, but his very existence has a bad moral
effect. In a community such as ours no position
ought to be other than elective. And while the
head of the Executive is nominated and appointed
by the British Government, wo have not attained
political freedom.” —The late liev. Huyk Gilmore
( Adelaide).
Signs are not wanting that the British
Primrose League lias passed its zenith and is on
the decline. It is not a very wholesome symptom
that the Grand Council should commence to
lecture the various habitations for their parsimony
in neglecting to subscribe to the funds of the
head office. No less than GOO habitations omit to
subscribe at all, and about 150 more send less
than =£l each. The fact is that many of the silly
old nobodies who love to sport an enatmlhd
primrose in their button-holes as if it were an
order of merit are dying off, and the younger
members of the League are beginning to see that
the funds are being expended in big salaries for
swells who can find nothing else to do but taek
themselves on to the staff for the sake of a living.
The League has clearly had its day, and a Liberal
majority at the next general election would give
it its finishing touch. Please don’t let Lady
Jersey know this—adds The Bulletin corres-
pondent.
Kingston, Q.C., last week moved the
second reading of his Conciliation Bill in S,A.
Parliament. The measure proposes that a Board
of Conciliation shall be appointed by the
Governor, to consist of three masters’ and throe
mens’ representatives, and a president to be
appointed by Parliament. The president to hold
office during good behaviour and to receive a
substantial salary. In any case of dispute, the
Minister of Industry first to investigate the nature
and extent thereof, and decide whether or not it
shall be referred to a State board. If yes, the
Governor next, by proclamation, to so refer the
question. The award of the Board to be binding,
and enforceable by a registrar. The Board to
have power to appoint preliminary committees of
settlement; also, the option of refraining from
making any award, and of publishing anopinioha-
tive report on the dispute instead. Local boards
to be elected, except in the case of the chairman,
who must be a man altogether unconnected with
industry, and appointed at the first meeting by
both employers and employees. Private boards
of conciliation, arranged by agreement, are also
recognised by the Bill. A Conciliation Hall to be
erected at the expense of (he country.
It is tolerably evident, writes The Bulle-
tin London correspondent, that Mr. Schnadhorst,
the British Liberal organiser, is only half-heart* d
in regard to direct labour representation The
report of the Liberal Federation, for which he is
responsible, throws cold water upon the scheme
by attempting to prove how very different circum-
stances are in England and Australia. Nobody
said they were not; but the Liberal wire-pullers
seek any excuse to back out of supporting direct
labour representation. Payment of Members,
they say, will be one of the planks in the new
Liberal platform, but they are careful to point out
that even when that point is achieved wc shall be
very little nearer to labour representation. It is
very clear that the party are glad enough to make
use of the working-man’s vote at the polls, but
they fear that as Members of Parliament the
workman may be apt to put labour questions
before party allegiance. So long as a constituency
can be fought by a rich man they are ready to
ignore a labour candidate; it is only where the
bloated capitalist has no chance of defeating the
Tories that a pro’etaire will be looked upon with any
favour. The Liberal Federation has gone even so
far as to chide the labour-party for presuming to
nominate at all when a Liberal is in the field.
“Save me from my Liberal friends,” should be
the working-man’s prayer. He has as little to
hope for from one party as the other. Patience,
good organisation and aggressiveness are his only
hope.

Personal Items.
Sir Julian Salamons is a large landed-pro-
prietor. Ho owns big farms in tropical N.S.W.
Tummy, according to a London paper, lias
ordered a wedding-present for Salmon Wilson’s
son.
Morehead is continually hinting in the
Bananaland Parliament at M'llwraith’s present
impecuniosity.
The solicitors of Madame Melba’s father,
under instruction, publish in the Argus a denial
of Madame’s guilt.
D’Arcy Wentworth has quite deserted
London. He lives at Brighton and has, they say,
taken up Theosophy.
J. H. B. Warner, who died in England
worth £131,800, was Private Secretary to Sir Win.
Jervois when the latter was Governor of South
Australia.
In addition to a pension of £250 a year
since 1877, the late E. H. Hargraves received a
bonus of £lO,OOO from N.S. Wales, and £2381
from Victoria.
The Bulletin wonders how much of the
,£11,500 being paid by N.S.W. Government for
Governor Phillip’s statute will be actually pocketed
by sculptor Simonetti.
Mr. W. H. Jameson, son of Sir John
Jameson, of the early Australian days, died
recently at Capertee (N.S.W.), the result of an
operation after an accident.
W. J. Jones, editor of the Bathurst
(N.S.W.) Free Press, died on November 2nd, of
influenza and bronchitis and —old age. He was
87, and had put in half-a-century in Bathurst.
Miss Maud Millett has several girl cousins
residing in Sydney. One of them used to bear a
striking resemblance to the actress. Their father
was formerly an officer in the P. & O. service.
There’s a proverb about a whistling
woman, and Miss Nellie Mitchell (Madame
Melba) was the most accomplished siffleuse at the
Melbourne Presbyterian Young Ladies’ College.
Varney Parkes, M.P., son of the original
Parkes, is very popular and much respected in the
N.S.W. Parliament. He is, politically, such a
contrast, morally and intellectually, to the old
man.
The Emperor of Germany has grown a
beard. This leaves his alleered mental derange-
ment a vexed question, although the evidence
points to the probability of William’s myrmidons
having hidden his razors.
Earl Clancarty, it is cabled, left our recent
„ visitor, his hopeful son, Dunlo, nothing but his
personal estate. Those who know the family are
aware that he had little else to leave, the family
being one of the poorest in Ireland.
Sir Alfred Stephen, though ill and weak,
turned out in East Sydney to vote for a member
of the bar, perhaps for the last time. He had to
be assisted into the booth. It is to be regretted
that he has by no means finished his auto-
biography.
It is not generally known that the late
Laurence Oliphant is a kind of connection of the
Macleays. His mother was the sister of Mrs.
Campbell, who was, in the early convict days,
private secretary or something at N.S.W. Govern-
ment House, Sydney. She was a Macleay.
The late Jules Gravy’s wife was, before he
married her, a buxom waitress in a cheap restaur-
ant much frequented by impecunious students in
the Quartier Latin. As every French soldier is
supposed to carry a Marshal’s bdton in his knap-
sack, so every bonne now feels the potentiality of
a home in the Elysee.
Major Benny-Tailyour, who is to take Col.
De Wolski’s N.S.W. military post, will, writes a
London correspondent, be a great addition to the
local cricketing talent. He is a grand hitter, a
good field, and can bowl a bit. In his time he has
made phenomenal scores against weak bowling.
He once played for Kent.
J. E. Moulden, one of the oldest of
Adelaide solicitors, died the other day. He is
best remembered as the hero of the religious fights
in Adelaide years ago, when the ritualistic
practices of one or two Anglican parsons created
a sensation. Most people sided with Moulden,
and the practices were gradually discontinued,
but Bishop Short never forgave his legal friend.
As Attorney-General of Vandemonia, Sir
Alfred Stephen was once most violently denounced
from the bench by Judge Montagu for eating
sandwiches in the Judge’s presence and delaying
atrial. “No. sir,” thundered the judicial rough
to the puisne Judge in embryo, “in your official
capacity I shall always treat you with the courtesy
and respect due to you. Were you elsewhere I
should treat you, after your conduct, with less
courtesy than a dog.”
Mr. Armstrong, Madame Melba’s husband
is an excellent boxer, and fought two battles to a
finish with Brisbane’s athletic solicitor. Arm-
strong won the first mill, but in the return match
his opponent went for A.’s body and let his own
face take care of itself. The result was that the
diva’s husband was so badly knocked out by a
body-blow that he went to bed for a fortnight,
while his slim conqueror took his painted face to
and from his office in a cab.
Matt. O’Shannassy is in London under the
chaperonage of J. R. Medley, who was formerly
an officer of N.S.W. police. “ Old Med.” is itdus
achates to most of the Australians who visit
England for a run, for he has the town at his
fingers’ ends, and knows a little about everything.
He saw Martin Loughlin safely through the
snares of Modern Babylon, and saved him from
the wiles of the Regent-street sirens.
Donald Gordon, the original owner of the
Mount Morgan Goklmining Co.’s freehold, is now
living on a miserable selection below the Main
Range, on the flat country towards Ulam, and is
“literally without the means to supply himself
with the bare necessaries of life.” Gordon, in his
time, owned Kyanga Station, near Banana;
Ellangowan and Jondaryan Stations, on the
Darling Downs ; a grazing farm on what is now
Mount Morgan township; and the freehold
already referred to. A diabolical run of bad luck
seems to have haunted his whole life.
“Jack,” the kangaroo pugilist, has died
of influenza.
In Queensland the aboriginal natives are
not allowed to travel free by railway. In N.S.W.
blackfellows get free passes.
“ Seeing is believing,” says Mrs. Besant,
on her messages from Madame Blavatsky. “ And
believing is seeing,” adds a gnarled critic.
A row is in progress in British Columbia
owing to the Chinamen there having got into a
depressing habit of killing their sick relatives.
They do this partly to. save worry and expense,
and partly for fear the disease should developo
into leprosy, and arouse an anti-Chow scare in
the land. How long the custom has prevailed no
one knows, but it only leaked out the other day.
An incipient leper kicked against the butchery,
and yelled so fearfully while his throat was being

“Braeside” Allan gravely told the Banana-
land Assembly the other day that, on theN.S.
W. side of the rabbit-fence, the rabbits were
lying dead piled-up in such numbers that the
squatters had to cart them away in order to
prevent the live ones from climbing on top of the
corpses and leapfrogging into Queensland. Allan
got incensed when hon. members “laffed ” at the
yarn.
Queensland and Maoriland wouldn’t agree
to a reduction in the cable-rates, and yet their
cable revenue has come down smash. During
the four months of ’9O affected by the change, the
Queensland cable revenue dropped from £8462 to
£3775 ; that of Maoriland, from £14,892 to £8639.
Explanation : Their cable-users mail their cables
to agents in the cheap-cable provinces, for trans-
mission. Wanted—Federation.
Things were pretty brisk in Melbourne
last Sunday. A despondent gentleman named
Brabazon drowned himself in the Saltwater River,
which is an exceedingly dirty place for any man to
drown himself in. An individual in the currying
industry took carbolic acid, and pegged out. And
an unknown person jumped into the Yarra, but
changing his mind on the subject he climbed out
again, and cut his throat instead. Two men were
killed by a boiler explosion in the harbour, and a
corpse—supposed to be that of Power, ex-bush-
ranger—was fished out of the Murray. It wore a
satin waistcoat, anyhow, and that’s what Power
used to do.
“ God’s Galatea.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
As she moved amongst the crowd in
Collins-street that summer afternoon a passionate
voice broke out above the low murmur of homage.
“ Look at that girl, Drummond ; isn’t she lovely!
Who can she be ? ”
“ She is so beautiful she might be God’s Galatea,
She had moved out of hearing.
“Or”
“ Or the devil’s advance-agent ! ”
But she looked very womanly as she stopped
suddenly before a show-window and said :
“Oli,” Agnes, look at that bonnet! Isn’t it
wretched to bo as poor as we are ? ”
“Yes, but tee can’t help it, dear,” replied the
pretty little woman at her side ; “besides you
wouldn’t care to give .£lO for it, would you,
Eily ? ”
“ Ten pounds ! Why, Aggie, I’d—l’d sell my
soul for it, I believe ! ”
“ Oh, hush, dear, that gentleman must have
heard you. Let’s go home; he’s staring so rudely
at you.”
# # *
A cold, grey wintry forenoon. Two
figures hurrying down a south suburban street.
“ Oh, constable, do you think she’ll be dead ?”
“No, miss; doctor guv her twm hours. Ain’t
been ’arf that time yet.”
“I wonder if I’ll know her. It’s over a year
since I saw her last.” ■
“ Mebbe ye’ll find changes, then. She’s been
terrible ill, mind ye, miss.”
They Stopped before a cosy little villa, and,
poised on tip-toe, she looked through the window
of a warm, cheery room for an instant. Then the
tears blinded her. She had seen so much in that
moment—so much that seemed to draw the other
world close down to her heart. Unconsciously
she found her way in, and flung herself on the
bed. Her pitying'eyes looked longingly, lovingly
down on a wan, white face. Such a sad, pinched
face. Such large, heavy eyes. And very little
over a year had passed.
“I knew you'd, come, Aggie how terrible
that weak, sweet voice—“ I waited for you, dear.”
“Waited, Eily?”
“Yes, yes. I wouldn’t go till I had seen you.”
“Perhaps you needn’t go at all, Eily—not fo*
years and years. ”
“Don’t think that, Aggie. God is kind some-
times.”
“ God is kind always, Eily.”
“To you, dear; to those who are good and—
and don't want any help.”
“Oh, Eily, how you said that! If you have
wanted help why didn’t you send to me before ?”
“ Because I wanted it.”
“ Because-1 don’t understand.”
“No, you don’t —you can’t. Neither could 112
months ago.”
“Ah, yes, 12 months ago. You will tell me —I
want to know why you left me.”
“ You haven't guessed ?”
“No.”
“ Hasn’t anyone told you anything ?”
“ Eily, dear,’don’t mind it—no one has spoken
of you since.”
“ And didn’t that tell you anything?”
“ Nothing at all.”
“ What did you think I went away for ?”
“ I thought you must have got lost.”
“ I did, dear. I did get lost—and —and that’s
why I’m waiting to go now.”
“ Eily, how strangely you talk. Won’t you
rest yourself, dear? You forget how vreak you
are. ”
“ And you don’t know how weak I have been.
Aggie, there isn’t much more time for me here —
I shall have rest enough presently, so I will tell
you why I went away from you,”
“ I don’t want to know, dear, if —if it hurts
you. ”
“ It has hurt me for so long that I hardly feel
it now, Aggie. You remember the last day we
were in Collins-street together ? and the gentle-
man who made a remark about me? Well, I
Aggie, I was wrong; it does hurt—l —l went
away with him.”
“ Oh, I know now, Eily. You married him
and he died, and—and —”
“Look, Aggie!”—she held up her left hand —
“ you know now.”
Yes, she knew then. Her kisses and her tears
answered for her till her trembling voice whis-
pered :
“Eily, Eily, you were too good for that !”
“Too good, Aggie! I wish God had thought so.”
“ But, Eily, where is—7ie?”
“He was here this morning—just come from
burying the child—”
“The child! Oh, Eily !”
“ And he sent the constable to you.”
“Then, he is kind to you?”
“Yes, he is kind —so kind that when I said
that I wished my child could have had a grander
funeral, he told mo not to complain, as he would
see that I was buried magnificently enough for
both !”
“ Oh, Eily, how dreadful! And he called you
‘ God’s Galatea !’ But, tell me —he was a stranger
—why —how did you go away with him ?”
“ Don’t you remember that bonnet I admired
so much ? ”
“ That bonnet! Yes, but ■”
“ Well, almost at the same time as I received
the bonnet, I got a note, asking me to meet him—
and I went! ”
“ But, Eily, Eily, I gave you that bonnet! ”
“ You, Aggie ! ”
“ Yes, dear—-oh, why didn’t I tell you—you
wanted it so much, you said you’d sell your soul
for it! ”
“ I might have said body and soul, Aggie.”
“ And I’d saved a little money, you know, and
—oh, Eily, can you ever forgive me?”
“ Hush, don’t cry, Aggie. It was a mistake,
dear—it can’t be helped.”
“ But, if I had only told you ! ”
“It would have been just the same. I wanted
so many pretty things then, and you could not
have always given them to me.”
The end soon came.
WALTER L’ESTRANGE.
Few Jews make money, but most of them
are terrors for annexing the money after it is
made.
Dear Bulletin, —W. T. Stead’s latest
journalistic psoriasis in the Review of Eeviewsls
a rash on ghosts. He states his position thus :
(1) he has never seen a ghost; (2) the existence of
ghosts is proved by the unbroken testimony of
mankind ; and (3) he would take a ghost-census
from among his readers. There is probably not a
man nor woman living who has ever been ill
enough to be light-headed, and who is endowed
with the least modicum of imagination, who has
not seen a ghost. If that doesn’t prove the
actuality of ghosts, it proves the existence of tem-
porarily unbalanced brains, anyway. And the
spectres exist —somewhere ! even if it be only
in a shatterpate’s fancy. The visions seen by
the jimjamee have a real existence for
him only. The unreal phantoms that escort
and attend the lunatic are veritably there
—as far as he is concerned. Who shall say
authoritatively that the cerebral eye “the
mind’s eye, Horatio” —can see a thing that has no
existence whatever, physical, spiritual, astral, or
psychic? To attempt to prove a negative is a
large order. It is all very well for a perfectly
healthy man to heave a brick at a ghost which he
thinks he sees, and find that it is a fellow dis-
guised as an apparition. I want to meet the man
who will heave an effectual brick at the spook
which he doesn’t see, but which I do. I will hail
such a man as a benefactor and borrow money
from him without shame. Further, can a horse
or a dog see a ghost, supposing a ghost to be
present? Some people say they can. I have
certainly seen dogs that were invisible to other
spectacles.—Yours, The Dipsomaniac.

The Bulletin has it, upon reliable
authority, that a certain minor Judge, whose
pecuniary difficulties have been so frequently
before the public, has been relieved of the duty of
presiding in civil cases, and will in future deal
only with criminal matters. Tims he will, to
some extent, be out of the reach of temptation.
# # #
Another Sydney club—the Southern=has
come to the hammer, after a tempestuous exist-
ence, during which it at least once changed its
name. It was but a few years old when it fizzled
out, yet most of its original members are now
either dead or “ dead-broke.”

Old David Mitchell, the Melbourne con-
tractor, father of Madame Melba, was very slow
to let her go on the operatic stage, but Madame
Marchesa pleaded that it would be a sin to keep
her off, and so he gave in.

Mr. C. H. Schon addresses the Kentish
Gazette (Canterbury, Eng.):—
Sir, —Having recently seen a summary of the will of
the late Dr. Beaney in your paper 1 was a little surprised
to find that although there were ricly benefactions to the
City bt Chntelbtlrj there was no mention of any provision
for some of his poor relations in the neighbourhood. I
have for many years known his only sister and family
andean vouch for the fact that they have been for years
in the receipt of “parochial relief !” One of Dr. Beaney’s
nephew’s—a man of weak mind—is at the present moment
an inmate of Bridge Union. The sister is a most respect-
able woman of about 70 years. I hope this may meet
the eye of somebody who will be able to get her into one
of the Canterbury Almshouses. The llev. H. Wardell,
Bekesbourne Vicarage, is conversant with the facts of
the case and will be happy to give any information
regarding it.
After this, is there any city, Church, of public
institution mean enough to touch a penny of the
dead butcher’s leprous money ?

From the Hawaiian Gazette (Honolulu),
9th June, 1891 :
NOTICE.
I THE UNDERSIGNED, hereby notify Mr. Hanale,
police of Kaupo, Maui, and Mr. Kike, of Nuu,
never to come near my dwelling-house, cook-house,
and outhouses. I cannot, nor do I debar them from
bntering my school-house, for they are patehtS and
have children going to school; nor do I debar them
from going as far as the letter-delivery box. But if
they ever dare to make approaches to my wife, as
they have done to two other married women-viz.,
entering the bedroom, giving them gin, embracing,
kissing, and the like, during the absence of the wives’
husbands, I will shoot them dead sure.
C. ANDREWS.
Kaupo, Maui, May 25, 1891.
There, now !

Mr. Jones, of Sydney, is alleged to havo
broken, with :t stick, the nose of his wife (who at

because she didn't keep his dinner in the oven.
This is one of the most beastly natural occur-

# » #
A Maoriland citizen recently charged with
pounding his wife, pleaded in defence that she
used to go to the Salvation Army five nights in
the week, and all day on Sunday, and leave him
desolate, and the house dusty, and allow the
miserable infant to run wild. The magistrate dis-
missed the case, from which we assume that he
would also have pounded his wife under similar
conditions. Hallelujah!

One of the leaders of Hot Tong, in a
Certain southern town wo know of, is a stout lady
with a great waste and wilderness of —ahem
chest. Her evening-dress is a trifle tight, and the
—not to put too fine a point upon it - chest slops
over the top of the dress, in a manner of speaking.
Consequently, any one meeting her for the first
time is surprised to find himself introduced to
such a very large amount of woman and such a
very small amount of clothing. The other day
there was a sassiety function of some sort in
progress, mid the lady in question, who is
a heavy eater, was consuming some jelly.
She tried to insert a No. 12 spoonful of the com-
pound into a No. 10 mouth, and the natural
result was that the provender fell. It lauded
right on her chest, and stayed there. The lady
looked at the empty spoon in search of the lost
eatable ; next she looked at tho floor to see if it
was there; then she fixed her blue eyes on her
chest, and discovered it. It was a trying moment,
and a thrill went through the crowd as she sat
there with the unstable provisions tottering on
their unstable basis. But she rose to the occasion,
and chasing the grub with her spoon she promptly
feboVered it. Then she gently raised the spoon
with both hands, opened her mouth to its widest
extent we are called away by important busi-
ness at this moment, and have to leave the narra-
tive unfinished.

LITTLE JOHNNIE : “Please daddy, give us some of that stuff?"
HIS FATHER: “What stuff?”
JOHNNIE: “Some of that there stuff.”
H. F.: “What there stuff?”
JOHNNIE (triumphantly)'. “Some of that there stuff that don’t make meat no good when
there ain’t none on it.”
(Johnnie alluded to the salt.)
A domestic servant, in the employ of a
well-known wealthy Sydney philanthropist and
Woman’s Rights man, left suddenly the other day
without receiving her wages. When she returned,
shortly afterwards, the amount was tendered
minus" half-a-crown, which, on enquiry, she was
told was deducted in payment for—one kitten.
It seemed that the old black cat which used to
haunt the potentate’s kitchen and back yard had
added to the feline population, and the girl had
given one of its progeny to a friend.
* * *
A great sensation has been caused at
Hobart by the fact that Dr. Arthur Bingham
Crowther familiarly known as “ Bing.”—is
accused of having resorted to malpractice in the
ease of a Miss Annie Jordon Cope. Reginald
Ritchie, a Launceston solicitor, is charged with
having aided, abetted and procured. The doctor
failed to answer to his bail, and the police say he
has left the island. It is only the other day that
Dr. Crowther had a critical operation for hydatid
performed upon him by Mr. Fitzgerald, of Mel-
bourne. ■
# * *
An observer who acts for the Government
Astronomer at a certain N.S.W. station recently
sent in an evaporation report. In reply, he
received from the Astronomer a very scientific
letter, pointing out that the air could not possibly
have absorbed the amount of water stated in his
return. Would he be so good as to elucidate the
matter? He presently discovered that the fowls
belonging to the next-door people used to hop up
on the roof and use the evaporation-recorder as a
drinking-trough.
# * *
The Gilbeys are now pushing the sate of
Australian wine in England, and it is alleged
that the firm of Burgoyno and Co., who
buy for them, have 200,000 gallons in stock in
Loudon. One of the most sensible moves yet
made to encourage the consumption of native
wines in this country, is the sale in some of the
Sydney restaurants of “ baby ” bottles of hock
and claret—each bottle containing an imperial
half-pint, and costing sixpence only.

Some time ago, at a meeting of Sydney
philanthropic ladies —the typical, well-dressed and
over-dressed hens’ convention—the wife of a poli-
tical hanger-on of the Great Statesman X. gave
notice that she intended to introduce Lady X. to
Lady Jersey. A row ensued, but tho politician’s
wife reiterated her intention, and the meeting
broke up in confusion. A day of two after this
the politician was elevated to the Legislative
Council.

A little podgy Government official out in
far north-West N.S,W, had his-small, fat dignity
trodden on the o'thef day, He got a telegram
from a policeman addressed simply “ J. Blank,”
and he rode 22 miles in search of the offender and
raised a tumult with him for not adding “Esq,”
The officer exp’aiued- that he had inserted the
“ Esq.” and it had been lost in tho telegraph-
office —which always loses the “Esq.”—and there-
upon the podgy one bolted for the telegraph
premises and demanded to know why the Gehenna
his “Esq. ” hadn’t been forwarded. The man in
charge said it had been left out to save time, and
the official said “Damn!” and demanded an
apology, and the operator told him to keep his
hair on. And now the back-blocks potentate is
writing long letters to the Government requiring
the instant dismissal of the telegraph-man, and
there is such a fearful row in the far north-west
that nobody dare even think of him without
putting “Esq.” to his name in his thoughts, and
the drunken bushman who swears at him says
“Dash J. Blank, Esq. !”
# * •
Toothbrushes are the insignia and cogni-
zance of gentility in Australian gaols, just as high-
necked dresses are the undoubted indication of
chastity in young society dames. No “silver-
tail ” of any ton to speak of is without one of these
little toilet appurtenances. It at once affords a
passport to the society of the bank-defaulter, the
land-boomer, the auctioneer who has sold crags on
heaven-kissing hills under the name of surveyed
allotments, and chevaliers d'industrie in general.
Some of those who polish their gums under the
taps were as dubious at first as to the real utility
of the toothbrush as was the American gentleman
who borrowed one for the purpose of beautifying
his toenails. Thus it is that in gaol may be
watched the evolution of the dude from the yahoo;
the demonstration of the thesis that culture pro-
ceeds from simian imitativeness. When an
expiree is on the eve of departure, he is besought
not to write his friends a letter from home, or to
perform any other sentimental eccentricity, but to
leave at the gate under the name of a relative a
new toothbrush, which becomes a marketable
medium of exchange for terbacker. A new tooth-
brush is worth three “figs” on the time-payment
principle.
1 * # * *
Though the Melbourne coal-supply has to
be imported from Sydney, the price of gas in
Melbourne (where the works return a big profit)
is ever so much lower than in Sydney. Why ?
Simply because gas-supply has always been a
monopoly in Sydney, whereas the Melbourne
prices have been regulated through long competi-
tion between companies which have now amalga-
mated. If Parliament pass an electric-lighting
bill in favour of the Sydney Gas Co. —or any
other private ring— it will be simply slitting the
public carotid. The lighting of Sydney should be
controlled by the municipality, and if the citizens
own the electric-light they can keep the gas com-
pany where it ought to be—right under the whip.
It is a recognised fact that everybody
should be very careful in selecting his parents; if
be isn’t careful, and rashly engages a father with-
out examining the old man’s credentials, he often
gets one who is utterly unfitted for the place, and
then there is trouble. A young and haughty nob,
in a Victorian Government office, who had given
two unpresentable people a job as his ancestors,
found out the irreparable nature of his mistake
the other day. He was the usual type of a nice-
looking Civil Service young man, with a shiny
hat and shiny boots, and a flower in his button-
hole; but his father was simply awful. The old
progenitor was short and stertorous and hairless ;
he used to perspire and steam on the slightest
provocation, and mop himself up with a cotton-
handkerchief ; his legs were curly, and his ignor-
ance was something phenomenal. Also, the
youth’s mother was objectionable, and flat-footed,
and they looked somehow as if they had made
their money by carting dirt on a large scale.
Therefore, the gilded son seldom invited his
friends home ; he had tried it once, and his cordial
father shook hands with the visitor till they both
perspired furiously with the exertion, and wanted
him to have a gin and a clay-pipe, and after that
he practically repudiated the curly predecessor,
and cast him off. But after a time the young
man was engaged to be married, and the
object of his passion wanted to see her
future mother-in-law. He staved her off
for a while, till at last he couldn’t postpone
the thing any longer. Then he had visions of the
brawny male parent squashing his nice girl in a
hairy embrace, and the mother —who looked
dreadfully like an apoplectic washerwoman—
pawing her with two big red hands ; and the idea
made him curse. While he was cursing, however,
a great inspiration came upon him—an inspiration
that made him give three cheers in his bed-room
for sheer exultation.
He rushed out and took a furnished
house to begin with. Then he hunted up a retired
actress of his acquaintance—a nice, elderly lady
with silvery hair —and engaged her to fill the billet
of his mother for the time being. Next, it was
decided that it would make the best girl feel more
at home if there were one or two attractive sisters,
so the ex-actress hired two of them on a cash
basis—one a theatrical girl out of an engagement,
and the other an ex-nurse from an hospital.
A MEMORY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Adown the grass-grown paths we strayed,
The evening cowslips ope’d
Their yellow eyes to look at her,
The love-sick lilies moped
With envy that she rather chose
To lake acreamy-pelalled rose
And lean it ’gainst her ebon hair,
All in that garden fair.
A languid breeze, with stolen scent
Of box-bloom in his grasp,
Sighed out his longing in her car,
And with his dying gasp
Scattered the perfume at her feet
To blend with others not less sweet;
lie loved her, but she did not care.
All in that garden fair.
The rose she honoured nodded down,
The others burst with Spite}
Toor fool, he knew not he was doomed
To barely last the night ;
Are hearts to her but like that flower,
The plaything of a careless hour,
To lacerate and never spare
All in that garden fair ?
I held her hand that I might trace
Her fortune in its palm ;
A holder moonbeam than the vest
Crept up and kissed her ami,
And kissing once was loth to leave,
So hid himself within the sleeve
That clasped the lithe arm, white and bare,
All in that garden fair.
I traced her fortune: love and wealth,
Tho’ life, alas ! was short,
But will that wealth be bought with love?
Or love with wealth be bought ?
I know not, knowing only this—•
Her hand seemed waiting for a kiss,
I longed to, hut I did not dare
All in that garden fair.
But she, alas ! is not for mo,
And I am not for her ;
Yet ever deep down in ray thoughts
A faint regret must stir
A thrill of longing—that among
Those moon-lit paths with lover’s tongue
1 might return, and woo her there
All in that garden fair.

Efforts Wore made to got a presentable father, bu
the only one who could bo found was a comic
Wan out of a show, and his expression, was sO
awful that it was considered better that the
father should be from homo Oil Urgent business.
A gentlemanly-looking individual from a Govern-
ment office arranged to call in casually during the
evening and be am Uncle, and the party was
complete. They made a nice looking family—
the sort of family any girl would bo glad to marry
into- and the nefarious scheme succeeded admi-
rably. Which shows, as already mentioned, that
the slop or ready-made parent is a dead failure,
and nobody should engage him, except after care-
ful inquiry as to his fitness for the job. The real
mother in this case would have hugged the girl
and breathed onions on her, but the temporary
one passed a white hand caressingly over her
soft, brown hair, and said she was pleased to have
such a daughter. The two bogus sisters wore
confidential with her, and kissed her joyfully;
the/illeged father sent a telegram, saying lie was
sorry he couldn’t gut home ; and the hired uncle
was immense* But how the thing will bo ex-
plained in the end, we really don’t know.

the Australian Court at the Sydney Art Gallery.
It is a pencil sketch, by Edward Rodins, of Henry
Parkes, taken ia the Criminal Court House, at
Darlinghurst, 14th February, 1864, during the
prosecution of some compositors of the Empire
staff for conspiracy.,

the prescription which cured him. Here it is *
Three liver pills, one shilling’s worth of quinine
00 drops of eucalyptus oil, one assorted drunk’
four cold baths, a long walk in the snn, one excur-
sion on the harbour, about 42 hard smokes
another liver pill, sixpenn’orth more quinine a
free fight, 20 more drops of eucalyptus oil,’ n
Turkish bath, and a row with his mother-in-law
All of these things to bo well shaken before taken!
In the case of a patient who hasn’t got any
mother-in-law a row with some other female may
be substituted.

The individual who said that man’s sin
would always find him out forgot to add that it
was quite sure to find him out at the most unsuit
able moment. For instance, a fashionable sort of
man got married the other day, in a fashionable
Sydney suburb, and he and his bride went to
their elegant home replete with all modern con-
veniences. But nearly half the night another
womati kept pounding ferociously on the back-
door and demanding to be let "in. She had
climbed over the back fence, and she positively
refused to climb out again, and for some reason or
other the bridegroom seemed to feel embarrassed
about sending for the police, and only ventured
to remonstrate feebly from the window. But
when he remonstrated the female below only
addressed him hilariously as “Johnnie,” and
urged hmi more strongly to let her in, and leaped
joytuiiy a t the door with a broom-handle. She
was a fattish sort of woman, with a blue dress
splashed with grease, and damaged boots, and a
hat with a broken-backed feather in it, and
altogether not at all the sort of female whom any
nn^ n -Y 0 ?.*" to have leaping and screaming
outside his back-door on his wedding-night. The
pride couldn t sleep on account of the racket, and
it wasn t till four o’clock that the visitor lost her
VOICe > and went away in disgust.

A certain great Sydney financier, well-
known to “Sir Henry,” used to peddle walking-
sticks and brummy jewellery (purchased under
the shoot by the bushel bag) on London Bridge.
Now he runs the Consolidated Bank of Ramoth-
Gilead, and says to impecunious Companions of
St. Michael and George, “Two thousand ducats
—for two months—well.”

A small Melbourne publication fell into
the droll error of supposing that the antediluvian
Marchioness of Ailesbury, who died the other
day, was the wife of the reprobate at present
•ngaged in pawning the family estates. As
a matter of fact, it was probably old Maria,
one of the other Marchionesses of A., who
yielded *up her worn-out ghost. With Maria
disappears the most extraordinary figure (not
even excepting the Duchess of Montrose)
seen on English racecourses during the last 25
years. Looking at this frisky fossil, it was diffi-
cult to believe that she hadn’t been bought in a
toy-shop and set going by steam. Hair, teeth,
complexion, eyebrows, figure—everything about
Maria was a caricature of human nature. She
looked 200 at the least, and yet she would hop,
skip, and jump around the royal enclosure
at Ascot, Goodwood, and elsewhere like a
marionette gone mad. Tummy used to tickle his
jaded senses by holding animated discussions
with this bag of antiquity, and when the old girl
was in her best form you might see her corkscrew
curls fluttering amongst the creme dc la creme of
sassiety. Maria was in truth the Mrs. Skewton
of the old nobility, an irrepressible scarecrow—
an object to laugh at, weep over, or write essavs
upon. Heaven and Sir Bernard Burke alone
know what relationship she held to the present
head of the family-probably that of grandmother
or great-great aunt. It is believed that she highly
disapproved of the young man, anyway, and had
of late refused to recognise him on earth. Con-
sidering, however, that Maria was herself n god-
less accumulation of pomps and Vanities, making
her betting-book her Bible* there is every reason
to suppose that she Will, ultimately find herself

This is th'd tale of a weak woman. A
Sydney mutual improvement society of two —to
wit a widow and a man —lately tried to run a
little paradise on the pristine Adam and Eve
unmarried lines. But the “Elysian theory worked
badly, and utterly broke down when the modern
Eve brought an action for wages as housekeeper,
and explained that her unlawful loVe had kicked
her out into the street. The unchivalric Judge
gave a Verdict for defendant, so outside the court
the persecuted widow went for the man she once
had fondly loved. With the very lips that a few
months ago had whispered the shy, sweet confes-
sion, she now swore to show him up and to tear
his heart out, fche broke his nose and blackened
his eyes with the delicate hand, which—oh, sad
remembrance I—he had pressed against his thrtlb-
bing heart, and had kissed, and had called his own.
She broke o’er his greasy but noble brow the
gamp that she had bought with “ pettie’s ’’money
the morning after the day when they should have
been married. Then she burst into tears, and sat
down on a doorstep and poured out her sorrows to
the cheap audience. She said she was only a
woman, and another victim of men, but it didn’t
matter because she only was a woman* and slm
hoped the Lord Would forgive him. Then she
commenced tb pray in a sing-song voice, and the
battered Adam started to shuffle down the street.
She followed, vowing that she loved him still, as
only a woman could, and that she’d never, never
forsake him. He didn’t look back, and she,
thinking that he might like to know where she
was, picked up hunks of blue metal* and let
’im ’ave ’em on the ’ed td apprise him of
her, Most women can’t throw
Straight, but this woman was on the wicke^
every time, and put ’em in with a force and
regularity suggestive of a thunderbolt, playing at
intervals of a second on a granite rock. With
one quarry she sent his cadie soaring heaven-
wards, and it landed on a verandah-roof, but he
was too stunned to miss it, and he hobbled on,
because it was the only iiotion he had. After a
while the back of the brutish master’s head began
to look as if it had been vanquished in a dozen
dog-fights, and ultimately he drifted a helpless
wreck through an open office-door, and fell down
in the hope that he’d soon die. She sat down
on the pavement to weep afresh, and after
fifteen minutes’ anguish, she arose, still sobbing,
and went to cook the dinner, and to make things
hot generally, in anticipation of the monster’s
return.

Amongst recent marriages, in England, of
people connected with Australia was that of Miss
Hose Mylrea, eldest daughter of Mr. F. Garland
Mylrea, for many years well known in Sydney,
where ho was long in business as a broker. He
married cn second noccs the widow of Peter
Stuckey, who owned considerable N.S.W. and
Victorian station property. She had two children
a son and daughter, by Peter, who were lost in the
wreck of the Loch Ard,”and the property re-
verted to her second family, who in this
unexpected way, came in for Stuckey’s money.
Mrs. Mylrea is a sister of Curtis Read, whose name
was a household word in the cricket world years
ago. Miss Mylrea’s husband is a Mr. Mayhew, a
Suffolk solicitor in large practice.

The late parson Hayden of St. John’s,
Darlinghlirst, Sydney, was an enthusiastic fisher-
man, alia spent a great deal of his tittlb afloat.
He used to fish outside Sydney Heads with a
man named W ,of Manly Beach, still extant,
who was a bit of a psalm-singer and amateur
preacher. _ They were on one occasion somewhere
on the wide ground, when a southerly buster
overtook them. It was a case of up kellick and
scud home, but the skiff being weighted with fish
laboured heavily, and was in some danger of
filling; W—— began to improve the occasion, and
reminded the parson of what a terrible thing it
would be were they suddenly called to their last
account. The rev. gentleman brought him up with
a round turn. “Look here, W , just attend
to your own business and leave the praying to
me. You look after the sheet. One parson’s
enough in the boat;”
• • 4 * ■ .
Mr. Hayden’s love for , the “ gentle
art ” was once the cause of considerable in-
convenience to two ladies. One of them, then
recently married, had invited a friend to spend
the day with her at Manly. It was proposed
that they should indulge in an alfresco bath, and
they went out for that purpose. Lunch time
arrived, but they had not returned. One o’clock,
three, four o’clock passed without sign of them.
The husband naturally became alarmed at his
wife’s long absence, and a search-party was
organised to scour thecdastJinei but uuavailingly.
In deep despair the husband returned ib tlib
bouse, where, to his joy, he found the wife and
her friend safe and sound. The spot they had
chosen for their bath was a secluded rock on the
Lagoon, where they undressed free from vulgar
gaze. They entered the water and swam some
little distance, when, to their horror, they saw a
man come close to the spot where they had left
their clothes. He proceeded to divest himself of
his iiether garments, keeping on only his coat.
He then Unpacked his fishing tackle and, having
hoisted ah Umbrella over his head, walked into
the Water ds far d& he fiould Without Wettihg the
tail bf his Coat. Here he remained for several
hours, whilst the girls shivered in the water
praying fervently that he would die suddenly or
go away. At fast, having exhausted iiis patience,
ho put on his togs and departed. That fisherman
was the Reverend Mr. Hayden.

At the present time great changes are
coming about in the social status of mankind, A
man is not nowa days Considered tl god; beballse
his father Was a clever usurer or merchant. It is
becoming a recognised fact that all men arc equal
—as men and have each the right to enjoy life’s
blessings to an equal degree. In the past it lias
always been the aim of the rich to grind down the
poor, and as the latter had but one
starvation—they were compelled to be thankful
for what they could get. But now education is
opening men’s eyes to the fact that mankind could
enjoy much more pleasure and comfort if such
large shares of the world’s wealth were not
annexed by the few. The day of a great indus-
trial revolution is at hand, and a brighter and
better future is in store for us both socially and
financially, and whilst paying attention to these
great interests it is also essential to take par-
ticular care of our physical well-being. An all-
seeing providence has so constituted the human
frame that the brain is immediately informed of
any organic derangement; a simple headache is a
sign that the liver is deranged, or that the nervous
system is weakened, which, if neglected, continues
to decline; the nervous tissues waste away and
completely collapse, and a fatal termination
frequently results. Herr Otto Franzen, Adelaide,
S.A., writes:—“ I was taken with a sudden fit
whilst dressing one morning last February, and
fell on the iron bedstead and cut my temple open
which bled profusely; the fall completely stunned
me, and I was unconscious for hours. My family
procured medical assistance, and I got somewhat
better in about a fortnight; but ever since the
accident I suffered tortures from nervous head-
aches and neuralgia and weakness. I tried all the
best doctors in this city and Melbourne for these
troubles, all to no purpose. I was almost dis-
tracted with pain, and applied to another doctor,
and he recommended Clements Tonic, saying that
my nervous system was prostrated with the shock,
and that Clements Tonic would rebuild my
constitution. I was rather doubtful, but procured
a bottle, and felt better with the second dose. I
continued its use, and am thankful I did, for I
truly believe I should have been in a madhouse
long ago if I had not used it, and I am really
grateful, for my sufferings were intense, and
doctors could not cure me, but Clements Tonic
did, therefore it deserves my gratitude.”
