One rebels against the sickly last words
reported of dying persons, but there is nothing
nauseous about the expiring words of a mother in
Port Melbourne last week : “ See that my boy gets
his dinner in time.”
Ex-Governor Carington recently told a
pleasant yarn about a time when he and Wales
went slumming together in St. Paneras :—
The landlord of the premises guided them into a room,
fireless and furnitureless, where a mother and three
children lived, the children being naked. The guide
asked the mother where the third was. “I don’t know.
I have not seen it since I was in court a fortnight ago,”
replied the mother, evidently quite unconcerned as to
the fate of her offspring. “ What on earth are we to do,”
asked the landlord, “ with people of this sort?”
The story ended there, from which it is evident
that Tummy, standing vacantly by, with his
corporation jammed in the doorway, gave the
problem up and went away to his little game to
baccarat.
“So long as I am a parson, so long will I
help the weak against the strong,” recently said
Rev. W. E. Hillier. Hillier had better come
right out of the fold. His brother shepherds
won’t recognise him if he adopts that programme.
What has it profited him, yet? For years he
toiled at Blackall without any certain stipend,
and lived on less than a Chinaman. The Church,
while taking to itself any credit he gained, ignored
if it did not disown him. Let him either grease
the fat pig and be a parson, or quit the white-
necktie contingent and be simply a radical re-
former. Run for Parliament, Hillier, if you like,
and, while being able to effect some good for the
masses there, you will be sure of your monthly
screw, at any rate.
According to Minister Cowley, of Banana-
land, a frequent trick of Polynesian pearl-divers
up at Thursday Island and adjacent ports is to
palm off upon passengers on mail steamers
“lizards’ eggs ” as “ pearls.”
Dear Bulletin, —Your Northern corres-
pondent is certainly correct as to the buffaloes
having found their way east to Queensland, but it
scarcely bears out his argument as to the tendency
or cattle to travel east, as he has omitted to
mention that buffaloes have also strayed from
Port Essington westward. I have seen more than
one pair of horns in native camps on the lower
Daly, and they have been seen on the Victoria.
I suppose as many have wandered west as east,
but coming eastward they got into more settled
country, and were consequently noticed. Stray
cattle, both in Northern Queensland and the
Territory, nearly always make south-east. This
miy arise from many of them having come from
that direction as “stores,” but more probably,
from the fact that the prevailing wind, for nine
months in the year, is south-east, and cattle, like
many other animals, when left to themselves,
always travel against the wind. — Delcomyn.
As showing the feeling which must exist
against the N.S. W. reserve rifle companies on the
part of the local military authorities it may be
mentioned that though the companies, all told,
now consist of from 5000 to 6000 men, each com-
pany, no matter whether its strength be 12, 20 or
100 members, is supplied by Government with but
twelve rifles. These are distributed by ballot and
the unsuccessful men have to use an obsolete
weapon (the Henry rifle) which the State issues, or
buy Martinis, at an expense of £2 18s. each. From
time to time it has been asked in Parliament why
Government refuse to supply rifles to the reserve 3 ,
and it is invariably replied that the province’s
stand of new arms must be kept up to a certain
figure. On the other hand, the senior cadets (who,
after all, are only boys) have as many good rifles
served out to them as they want. They have only
to ask and the arms are immediately provided.
Notwithstanding all the obstacles put by Govern-
ment in the way of the reserve rifles, these com-
panies are the real strength of the province, and
carried off most of the prizes at the recent rifle-
meeting;. They have to spend on an average
£6 for rifle and uniform, and all that is given by
Government is £1 for efficiency, which benefits no
individual but goes to the company’s common
fund.
One old friend and patron was dying last
week and wished to bid us an eternal farewell.
So we went to him and found him lying in a
darkened room, his once round and ruddy face
thin and ghastly, collapsed, waxen; his breath
coming and going in gasps and sobs; his eyes
faded ; his feet swollen with the dropsy which
firoclaimed the failure of his brave old heart ; his
ips half-paralysed; his teeth showing as show
the teeth of the dead man in his coffin : his pallid,
wasted hands lying nerveless outside the coverlet.
“I am sentenced to death,” said he, “and I am
glad to die. I have come to the lane which has
no turning, and don’t care how soon I get to the
end of it.” “Well, old fellow,” we said, knowing
him to be one who looked the inevitable squarely
in the face, and on whom honeyed words of hope
would be wasted— have little cause to
grumble. You have the consolation of knowing
that you leave your family fairly well pro-
vided for.” “That’s all right!” exclaimed the
dying man, “so I have, but it has been at the
expense of hard work and never-ending worry—
even up to now none of them have even been able
to relieve me for one instant of business care.
My youngsters will get a good start, but,” —here
he fairly snorted with disgust—“ they are no
f:ood. Not one of them have ever had to work
ike you and me, and the result is that not one of
them is worth his salt—no go or energy in them—
an utterly useless lot, except Michael, that boy ”
—(pointing to a curly-haired youth of seventeen,
who sat reading at the other side of a window).
“ Michael is an exception.” “ Ah, well,” we said,
“ they are none of them very old yet, and it must
console you to know that you have one, at least,
one good boy.” “Yes,” said the dying father,
with a grim smile, “Michael is the exception.
The rest are spendthrifts, wasters, fools, for which
the evils of the day are sufficient. Michael is a
provident boy. He saves, saves, saves, spends
nothing during eleven months out of the twelve.
He is always saving, saving— saving, saving up
for the Melbourne Cup ! ” And thus ends the
story of the dying Australian father.

Some years ago a certain spiritualist-
lecturer (now a prominent politician) was to give
in Sydney a trance lecture on “ England’s rela-
tions with Turkey.” After all the preliminaries
had been gone through and the lecturer had been
duly switched on to the medium, the chairman
arose to explain that the young man whom they
saw before them was now in communication with
somebody in spirit land, and was completely un-
conscious and oblivious to all external sensations,
etc., and that he would now proceed to give his
trance lecture on “ England’s relations with
Russia.” “No, no, hang it, man, not Russia,
Turkey!" ejaculated the medium—the man who had
gone out of himself. The audience “bit” and
that lecture didn’t come off.
How often have we mashed the girls,
And deemed them bond fide pearls,
Only to find
Their teeth were false, their busts were bogus,
Their hair and bloom combined to rogue us,
Their wealth a blind.
All bow’d and pigeon-toed their legs ;
In fact, the “ pearls” were “lizards’-eggs !”
But never mind!
We’ll try again to find a pearl,
Some day we'll get an actual girl,
Without a rind.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
If the Melbourne Cup were to be run over
again next week the ’hooks would probably lay 5 to 2
against Malvolio repeating the trick, seeing that he
proved unable in the All-Aged Stakes to make a conces-
sion yf-nine pounds on their original handicap to High-
born, thelourth in the Gup. No line of reasoning can
make the form ’look quite up to the average, or upset
the aI gu merit that Carbine, fit and well, would have
donkey-uclted this year’s field with the same ease as of
!Py>rt|i»R- writers, indeed, have represented
&ir Wilhaih as a great performer, and even maintained
that, with Bravo s weight on his back, he was the jMck of
the handicap. It is difficult to understand, save under a
moat sinister theory, how a somewhat cobby horse, with
only lour -wins, as against sixteen “ nowheres,” to his
oredit in ths calendar, could be a good thing for the
biggest vw&vtthf y and as Sir William was a close
ninner-up, the inferenge is that alvery shag-bag or ill-
conditioned lot of animals(always excepting Strathmore,
the and Highbolrn, who carried 35 pounds
niore, Vet gbt iiearer to* tjie wiWer, than last year) must
have finished behind him.’ Strathmore, again, scored a
third i” than Australian Peer did in Dunlop’s
ygar. Nine people opt of ten, We fancy, will be disposed
J O ,. ink The Peer, with Niagara and Abercorn
behind him, a more brilliant Derby winner than
Strath more-ergo, Dunlop, at Bst. 31b., was a better horse
than Malvolio at a pound more. As for Mentor, another
Bst. 31b. Cup winner, he had previously won the Caulfield
Stakes, and run second for the Melbourne Stakes two
races in which Malvolio finished far behind Marvel and
Bungebah. How could backers put their whole trust in
the son of Malua and Madcap, except on the strength of
his trainer’s opinion, which has been proved fallible many
a time and oft? Malvolio, as a three-year-old, beat High-
born at an advantage of 91b. in the weights, which was
something to stand upon. But then, a week later, Car-
bine gave the one-eyed veteran a more decisive beating
and 391 b. into the bargain. However, no matter what
may be the exact degree of excellence in the “form ” one
good old moral adorns the story of 1891 Melbourne Cup.
Only racehorses, and not ordinary-looking animals with
no proved merit to recommend them, are worth backing
for the event. Malvolio was a well-bred and imposing-
looking animal anyway, the most likely winner to the eye
that walked in the paddock before the race. Sir William
had run, at one time or another, well over all distances,
and under heavy weights, whilst Strathmore represented
the “crack” of his year. They were, at least, three
“right uns, and they gave touts and prophets another
lesson on the vanity of running after second-raters of the
Pi geo n toe stamp at the last minute. Melbourne sporting
papers, printing Anal selections before the race, broke out
in veritable rashes (suitable word) of “ plumps ” for
Pigeontoe, a plebeian monster with deformed fore-legs,
who was correctly reckoned up in The Bulletin’s report
from Melbourne, after he had won the Hotham Handicap.
« * *
Paris had gone considerably “ off-colour ” before
The Bulletin tip was published. Under the circum-
stances, his foremost position for 1J miles in the Cup and
subsequent victory in the Veteran Stakes were a surprise
to many people. There was a flat, stale, and unprofitable
appearance about the horse all the week.

The Teksutn feat of last Cup Day was beaten
when St. Blaise won the same two races, and Quality
was second for both of them.

Mr. Silver King Wilson’s party dropped their
money oyer She, a mere pony of a mare, for the ,Oaks, and
didn’t win it back again when La Tosca carried the colours
home in front, an hour or two later, for the Spring Handi-
cap. Stable “ know ”’edge is often enough the darkest
of ignorance.

Redcap, a brother to Malvolio, ran unplaced
and unbacked in a race at one of the Melbourne suburban
meetings last week.

When Malvolio was a yearling he had a very
bad skin-disease, and his owner offered him to a sporting

* * *
In their schools, it is rumoured, the Hebrews
teach their little boys the names of the Cup winners
backwards along with those of the kings of Judah.
* * #
Joe Thompson, writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent from London, has been waking up the Victoria Club
and recalling old times by the extent of his wagering on
future events. The “jockeys’ ring “ had nearly done for
ante-post betting, and most of the business for the last
three years has been S.P. Joe, however, determined to
change all that, and accordingly opened big hooks on the
Leicester Handicap, the Ciosarewitch and Cambridgeshire
He laid any number of wagers in thousands. His pluck
has already been deservedly rewarded, and he has had a
fair “skinner” over the Leicester event, which was won
by an unbacked outsider, Rusticus, the property of that
unlucky sportsman, Mr. Hamar Bass. Joe, who must
have landed a very large sum over that win, is also doing
a big business in double events on the two big back-end
handicaps. He is not adding to his popularity with the
English bookmaking fraternity by doing this.
* # #

WILLIAMS, THE AUSTRALIAN CHAMPION LIGHT-WEIGHT.
They were partners in a pub. at Newmericarra,
and introduced the seductive game of hazard. The
hoys thought it considerably better than Yankee grab
especially as they had been winning. But one fatal
night, when there was a big school on, the partners
rung in a clyner,” and the way the boys went down was
dreadful to see. One battler, after droppinglall he had,
bet on the nod to the extent of £3O. On being pressed
for a settlement he handed over the title-deeds of a town-
ship allotment. Just then Father Mick called and
solicited a donation for the bazaar. “ Let’s give him the
block of land, Jack which was readily assented to by
the other partner. The Holy Fathers were so piously
elated that they invited the publicans to the presbytery
in the evening to have a glass of wine. A game of nap
was suggested, just to pass away an hour, you know. . .
“How much did that allotment cost us, Jack? £3O.
And how much did we win at the presbytery last night ?
£6O. Well, I think I’ll go to Melbourne to-day and give
Nation an order for a special brew.”
* * *
Recently the Age whined that no less than
£20,000 per annum of Victorian money was sent out of
the province for investment in Sydney sweeps. The post-
office authorities never suggest the possibility of any
money coming back to the unhappy Cabbage Garden in
the form of prizes. Why, the number of Melbourne and
Ballarat men who have just won fortunes, according to
rumour, in Sydney sweeps is positively legion. The same
remark applies to Sydney and most other towns. Read-
ing newspapers for a few days after the Cup always fills
the human mind with a hearty disgust for toil and
trouble. Why work when Tattersall and the rest of Aus-
tralia’s benefactors are waiting to throw untold gold at
everybody who buys a ticket of admission to Tom
Tiddler's ground ? Better to lay out your sovereign and
retire from business on your good luck.
• # *
The Olympia Club, New Orleans, offer £5OOO
for a fight between Sullivan and Slavin.
* * *
In Melbourne they are showing a photo, of old
Commotion, taken a few weeks ago. He is very dilapi-
dated, with his knees bent like a used-up cab-horse, and
all bandaged.
* * *
F. O’Rorke, a fireman on the Victoria railways,
nicked the double on the Caulfield and Melbourne Cups
by picking G’naroo and Malvolio six months ago, and
getting 1000 to 1 in sovs. When G’naroo collared the
Caulfield he laid off £lOO against Malvolio, so that he
raked in £9OO finally, and stood to win £9 anyhow.
* * *
The English lady cricketing team has, we read,
collapsed. The promoter started them with salaries of
355. to 60s. a week, but early in the tour the rate fell to
3s. a week, and in the end they had to subsist “on bread-
and-butter and a pennyworth of nuts for the whole day.”

Bad pins seem to run in the Trenton family.
The great Maorilander himself was always delicate in
that direction, his brother Cuirassier has for a long time
been an impossible animal to wind up, and now his half-
brother Zalinski has gone prematurely cronk.

“Look here!” hiccuped the owner of a Mel-
bourne Cup horse last week—“just you put yer—hie—
shirt on my moke. We’ve been runnin’ him stiff for
fifteen monthsh 1” And it nearly came off.
**9
Joe Thompson, writes our correspondent, ought
surely to receive a testimonial from English backers, fo r
the other bookies have fairly “ taken the needle ” since
he commenced to show them what Australian mettle is.
Men who would open their eyes if asked to lay odds to
a couple of thousand will now stand to any amount. Joe
laid at Leicester the big wager of £33,600 to £2400
against the French colt, Ermak, for the Csesarewitch.
It is said that Joe would have to cover no less than
£lOO,OOO should Chesterfield win the Csesarewith, so
heavily had that colt been backed in double events. (One
of the papers prints a story that Joseph dropped £36,000
over the Csesarewitch meeting.) Brother Barney is much
smaller potatoes. He is a simple turf-commissioner, but
is doing pretty well at that elastic game. He has a good
office in Jermyn-street, right in the heart of St. James’,
where most of the sporting Johnnies have chambers.
There he dispenses liberally whisky-and-soda and cigars
to all comers.

only once unplaced.

* * *
A certain N.S.W. small boy—aged
11 years—is in trouble over the Caulfield
Cup. He got the straight tip that G’Naroo
was the coming animal—according to his own
story he found his school-teacher backing the
horse, but this is a mere detail. Thereupon
he went straight home, buist his money-box
with an axe, and put the contents on G’Naroo
in one act. When he won, and collected the
proceeds—some £3O or so—he grew alarmed
lest his enraged parent might drop to the
state of the money-box, so he laid the matter
before his big brother, and asked him to
repair that receptacle. The brother repaired
the box, put back in it the original amount,
whaled the young speculator on high moral
grounds, and' confiscated the winnings with
a promise to pay them over when the
spanked and weeping owner should have
reached the age of 21. So far as we know
the big brother isn’t going to pay any in-
terest on the money, so that, through his
morality, he gets the use of £3O for ten
la , gratis. Also, there is no security,
and the way the coin was extracted looks
remarkably like assault and batten', high-
way robbery, and undue influence with
violence. The stern parent or the moral big
brother who confiscates the small boy’s
gambling-money from purely religious
motives is a mean cuss, anyhow, for he
always sticks to the cash, and always snuffles
about it as if he had done a noble act in
taking the battered infant’s property.

The Old Man’s Welcome.
(A Sequel to “ The Shame of Going Back ” in a
recent “ Bulletin.”)
So you’ve been a bit unlucky, and your clashed hard up,
And you blame “ Humiliation ” and his “ poisoned cup ’’;
And “your heart is in a shadow?” Why, you must be
drunk !
Light the fire and boil the billy while I fix your bunk.
There's very little doing here,"but don’t yon fret:
There is plenty junk and damper in the gin-case yet.
What tlie devil set 3-011 rhyming ? Your’re a damn’d fool
Jack !
Never talk about the sorrow of a coming back ;
You have had a lot of trouble, but the “pome” was
worst,
And you never thought of coming- to the old man first.
You’ll deserve the “frowns of Fortune,” you’ll deserve
her licks,
If you go without a dinner while the o’.d man kicks.
I am mighty glad to see you, for the boys are gone,
And it’s lonely in the humpy when the night comes on ;
There’s a pipe and good tobacco on the corner shelf,
There’s a sprinkle in the bottle you must help yourself.
I will hunt .you up a billet, Jack, but don’t forget
That there’s plenty junk and damper in the gin-case yet.
Sydney, Oct., 1891.
HENRY LAWSON.
Old Tom Gardiner, who died the other day
at Hobart, Ta*., at the age of 92 years, first
arrived in V.D.L. in 1820, per ship Guildford, and
got a billet as valet to Col. H.ivey, the then
Governor. Gardiner was afterwards Chief Justice
Pedder’s “ man,” and, later still, slid into a billet
as court crier, which position ho “Oyez’d”for 40
y<an, till dimness of sight and other infirmities
stiauded him on to the pension-list.
There is to be erected in Broadway, New
York, U.S.A., what will be the highest building
for ordinary business purposes in the world. Its
altitude will be 550 feet, a height only exceeded
by two monumental structures, the Eiffel Tower,
in Paris, and the Washington Monument. The
edifice will be constructed throughout of steel and
will contain exactly 1000 offices, distributed
amongst a huge pile of storeys too trouble-
some to count. The building will contain a
solid bottom —be constructed, in fact, to form
one jointed whole—which will give scope for the
ingenuity of artificial sensations, or for the
humorous effects of passing earthquakes. Thus,
if the mechanicians can produce screw-jacks
powerful enough to lift the structure bodily, it
can be “unevened” to represent the Leaning
Tower of Pisa, or it can be lassooed near the top
and hauled over on to its side, or caravaned upon
wheels and carted out of the jurisdiction of muni-
cipal taxes, or swung from a leviathan crane and
fitted with clockwork to go as a pendulum, or
weighted on to a mammoth balloon and sent
cloud dispersing, or electrified into one huge
magnet and made to gravitate through all
obstructions into the centre of gravity, or
Police Magistrate Pinnock, of Brisbane, is
a dear old soul—a human shrine of chivalry—and
is remembered as exhibiting real pluck in the days
of his “colonial experienoe-ing.” Occasionally he
fights his young battles over again on the bench.
The other day he thus delivered himself of his
pent-up disgust anent a trivial case: “When I
was a boy I turned on any lad who interfered with
me and gave him a thrashing. But boys nowa-
days lay an information and bring other boys who
strike them into Court.”—

THE VERY BEST.
A singie pain is often the first indication, and
the last throe, m one, of a physical climax, and
the climax is death. That St. Jacobs Oil has
again and again wrought its miracles just in the
nick of time, has bridged over the critical
emergency, and has
SAVED MANY LIVES
at the point of death, is fully attested by the
record of many extraordinary cures in which this
fact of saving life is duly set forth.
If to its remarkable virtues in the permanent
cure of chronic cases of Rheumatism, Neuralgia,
Lumbago, Sciatica, after years of endurance,
wherein everything else proved valueless, fortunes
were spent in vain, and crutches and crippled
limbs were the manifestations, is to be added its
supreme sovereignty over pain, even unto death,
it is wholly entitled to the appellation of
THE VERY BEST.
Its specific action is especially adapted with a
curative penetration and soothing effect to arrest
any pain at the acme of its worst influence.
A MOST REMARKABLE CASE
can be cited in proof, which is thoroughly verified.
It is in its main features taken from a leading
Liverpool journal as follows : tf Two. years ago ”
states Mr. William Buchanan, residing at No. 8,
■ John s-road, Kirkdale, Liverpool, and twenty-
four years in the service of the Cunard Steamship
Co., _ I was taken down with a most excruciat-
ing pain in my head, which was followed by
ONE YEAR OF TORTURE.
All physicians believed that it would lead to
softening of the brain and death. Six of the most
eminent attended me ; but gave no relief. I had
to be held down, and my screams were heard out-
side. My case was deemed incurable. My wife
procured a bottle of St. Jacobs Oil, and applied it
to my head. It acted like magic. I was cured
by it, and am now in perfect health, and I have
not had the slightest return since I was cured
some months ago.” Nothing more need be added.

PROGRESS IN CIVILISATION.
Nature never makes a mistake. Whatever
material is at hand is turned to the best use.
Man, the creation of Nature, follows in her foot-
steps, and steadily seeks a higher plane by re-
jecting the less worthy, and selecting that at his
command which is the most practical. This
progress in civilisation is marked in all details of
Ine, and particularly in the different apparatus or
mechanical contrivances for every day con-
tingencies. One by one the less fitting drop out
or are literally crowded aside by those offering
Mipenor advantages. _ Steam has superseded sail.
Ihe old time horological contrivances are pushed
to the rear by the newer, less costly, and more
reliable appliances of to-day. To-day the public
demand economy, combined with quality
honesty, and accuracy, and in all these particulars
the W aterbury watches “fill the bill,” and there-
by have gained respect and admiration in every
quarter of the earth as honest, reliable, and faith-
ful timekeepers, produced at as low a cost as is
compatible with good material, skilful workman-
ship, and their undoubtedly excellent qualifi-
cations.
Owing to a tremendous gust of wind at
Silverton on Oct. 29, the local Wesleyan Church
was completely wrecked. The pub. still proudly
stands. The world spends more in building its
pub. than it does over the erection of its church.
Writes a Bulletin correspondent:—
The greatest drawback to the progress of the Richmond,
is the state of the entrance to the river. The Government
are spending a lot of money, and are getting a poor
return for the outlay at the breakwater and training wall,
where they have about 30 bosses to look after 10 or 12
labourers. But it was ever thus on Government works.
The Lismore-Tweed railway won’t pay 1 per cent, on cost
of construction til] large vessels can enter and leave the
river at any time and in any weather. But the said
railway will be a white elephant anyhow for the next 20
y ears - ft represents the price paid by the people of
N.S.W. to buy the votes of 3 members who were
returned to further the cause of Protection, but who
were bought to vote for Preetrade b}* the granting of the
railway. And at least one of the aforesaid members will
get an unpleasant surprise next election.
At Aldershot, ling., a month or two back,
the body of a man was found with the throat cut.
A woman named Gilbert positively identified the
remains as those of her husband, and as a corro-
borative fact she stated that he had a deformed
finger. The dead man had such a deformity,
two Well-known respectable residents pf Alder-
shot swore to the body being that of Gilbert
whom they had known for niany years, and other
witnesses also believed the body to be that of
Gilbert. A few days ago the supposed dead man
walked into his wife’s house much to that, lady’s
astonishment. _ This strange case has made a
great sensation in the district. And no wonder,
yet the evidence as to the dead man’s identity
was conclusive from a judge-and-jury point of
view.
Dear Bulletin When the late N.S.W.
Poasbmasther-General came up to Monaro last
election the natives had some fun with him,
begorra. Ye see, Daniel had been swimming
several creeks and getting wet to the shkin while
going round the counthry, and the excoited pork
butcher was followed by a mob of about 96
admirers, mostly named Gallagher and Hoolan
a j i u f roone ya and they were all wet too. By-
and-bye they shtopped at a pub kept by a man
named Murphy, and while they were dhrinking
there a man named O’Brien was seen doing some-
thing to the Poastmasther's horse —I was informed
of this afterwards by a man named O’Grady.
Anyhow, after a while a man named Hooligan
helped the Poastmasther on to his horse, and the
procession started, and just five minutes after-
wards the Poastmasther was yelling at the top of
his voice : Whoosh! Woa! Gallagher! Doolan !
Mulrooney ! Shtop, for the love of Heaven, me
horse is upside down.” I ran out to see what the
rbw was about, and some son of a gun had turned
Dan s saddle back to front, and there was a man
named 0 Toole laffing himself to death behind a
haystack. I send this letter along to your office
by a man named O’Connell.—Yours, &c., Patrick
Donovan.
Gilbert Dalziel, proprietor of the inane
and truly English Ally Sloper’s Half Holiday,
recently offered a prize of £lOOO for the best verse
which might be sent in on a certain date. A Mr.
George Hicks— must be related, judging from his
literary quality, to the giant Hicks, erst of Sydney
Punch —won the money with this :
There was an old fossil named Sloper,
Astronomer, sage, horoscoper,
He fell down the stairs
And saw comets in pairs,
That unfortunate, fruity, old Sloper.
Fancy a man getting £lOOO for that! And yet
there* are hundreds of poor, clever devils knocking
about 1 leet-street who would be glad to get half-
a-sovereign a column for really decent verses. This
was no bogus business. Dalziel paid the money
right enough. The literary market is an inscrut-
able institution. Why, a Sydney newspaper-
proprietor can, for one £25-prize, get enough
competitive” stories to run his rag from one
Christmas to another.

Ellen Terry swings in a hammock between the acts.
Vincent West—his real name was Paul Washington Vin-
cent, and he was a son of the distinguished Chartist
of that name—late manager for the Sheridan Comedy
Company, died from typhoid at the Hobart General
Hospital, on Sunday, November Ist. The deceased, who
was a thoroughly good fellow and a smart business
manager, was only 2C years of age. His loss will be
deeply felt by Johnny Sheridan, after whose departure
for Brisbane West fairly lost heart, and remained in_a
very low condition until his demise. Poor West had, in
Brisbane, a severe attack of brain-fever after his troubles
with the insolvent Wangenheim Co.
Melbourne has just had another instance of the craze
for amateur acting proving stronger than the tie which
bound the lady to her luvin’ husband and infant.
Charles Wheatleigh, the old Australian favourite, who
brought the “Colleen Bawn” and “Shaughraun” here,
is with Daly’s company at London Lyceum.
Christie Murray’s assets consist of part of a play—
“ . . . the lot austere
That ever seems to wait upon
The man of letters here.”
At the Dundee theatre lately, they gave Hamlet a
carved turnip for Yorick’s skull.
Pinero’s new' London play is to be printed and circu-
lated amid the audience.
Both the Marguerites in Melbourne are bolsters.
H. H. Vincent acts Dr. Sutcliffe, in “ School," at London
Garrick, with young Irving and young Hare.
Now, if George Coppin would only come out as Bailie
Nicol Jarvie, in the red-hot poker business?
Bentley’s crack parts include Hamlet, Othello, Macbeth,
Richelieu, Shylock, arid Charles Surface.
Dampier’s Christmas Pantomime, at the Alexandra,
“ Jack the Giant Killer,” is to run “Dick Whittington ”
close.
Henry Irving, jun., is a failure up to date.
Geo. Vandenhoff writes, in his Memoirs, of how painful
it was to act with his father, because the old man had
determined he shouldn’t go on the stage—so with Kean,
Jefferson, Irving, and many more.
Henry Arthur Jones, in his new theatrical venture at
London Avenue, is proceeding in no niggardly spirit.
Witness the list of those whom he has already enlisted
under his banner—Olga Brandon, Winifred Emery, Lady
Monckton, Arthur Cecil, Lewis Waller, Cyril Maude, Sant
Matthews, to say nothing of other high-priced people.
This may be called a phenomenal salary list.
It must have been a very painful task, says Tle
Bulletin’s London correspondent, for Clement Scott and
the other Irving-wnrshipping critics to have to write
down the first appearance of their idol’s son as a dead
failure. Fortunately for them, young Hare’s debut, at
the same time, was a distinct success, and this served as
a cloak to the other, for in praising him they were able
to avoid saying much about young Irving’s lame perform-
ance in “ School.” Hare, junior, has undoubtedly a
brilliant theatrical career before him, it he be not spoiled
by undue adulation. There is just a chance that young
Irving may do better in heavier parts, for his style is
ponderous. The w'orst part of the youth is his voice,
which is harsh and unpleasant, and apparently unman-
ageable. I am told that Irving, pere, is much dis-
appointed that his son should have done so poorly. He
himself was playing at the Grand Islington the evening
his son appeared at the Garrick, and his anxiety and
abstraction were quite marked.

A row amid the extra ladies of the Melbourne Victoria
Hall came into court last week, and wouldn't it be better
to have none ?
This Alfred Tennyson Dickens, the lecturing son of his
father, is a spruce, stout, middle-aged gent., of Collins-
street appearance, with a big sprig in his buttonhole.
Jefferson is a most melancholy man, and has said, when
dressed for “ Rip Van Winkle.” that it was impossible for
him to act, in such a horrible frame of mind ; but they
shoved him on, and, of course, he acted better than ever.
How much does Orleans get for this job ?
Since Dampier has been playing “ Robbery Under
Arms,” about 18 big, stalwart, grey-bearded men have
called on him mysteriouslj', and refused to give their
names at the door, and have taken Alfred aside in a dark
corner, and looked anxiously round to make sure that
nobody was listening, and then secretly breathed into his
ear that they—all of them—were collectively and indivi-
dually the oply original Captain Starlight. Then they
have whispered hoarsely that their safety was now in
Alfred’s keeping, and that he possessed their life’s secret
—lB of them. After that they have softly asked for 18
separate half-crowns and 18 drinks. Dampier is a long-
suffering man, but he declines to stand this any longer,
and the next reformed bushranger who visits him will be
treated coldly. The real, original Captain Starlight is
now in Sydney, and will visit him shortly.
Launceston (Tas.) people lately received a great shook
in the death of J. A. James, the best amateur conductor,
next to Fred. Packer, in Tasmania. He was a musical
enthusiast, and in more than one capacity had been
associated with three Tasmanian newspapers. Another
prominent Launcestonian who has just died was William
Bull, who carried on the old Tam O’Shanter Inn for about
40 years. He could tell many a stiff yarn about hunting
Brady, the bushranger. His Inn was so called from its
sign, the figurehead of the ship Tam O’Shanter, lost near
14-mile Bluff.
One night during David Christie Murray’s Brisbane
lecture-season so few attended that the manager stood up
and said that as D.C.M. could hardly be expected to talk
to so small an audience the money would be returned at
the door. Then, in the middle of the hall arose a man
who groaned; “And how about the (adjective) bus-
ares ? ”

poodle.

Until now the Sydney postmen’s new
uniforms have been served out before 9th Nov.,
and it used to be compulsory for the men to begin
wearing them on Tummy’s birthday. This year
they have not yet been served out —another sad
proof of declining respect for the Crown.

Mr. Benzon, better known as the
“Jubilee Plunger,” has had a windfall. In the
days of his prosperity he gave an impecunious
friend <£16,000. Shortly before his death this
friend came in for some money, and he has now
returned the amount bestowed upon him by The
“ Plunger,” per bequest in his will.

“Square.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Twelvemonths ago I took an opportunity
of studying the Sydney shop-girl in her habitat.
For a dreary period of four or five weeks’ length
my wife and I endured existence in a Surry Hills
boarding-house which also accommodated five
other boarders—girls of the shop and the work-
room.
They were average girls—typical of their class
in manners, in the dress that aped that of women
with money in their purses, in the narrow and
narrowing thoughts—typical, alas ! in the pathetic
colourlessness of their lives, across whose lustre-
less days and nights there shone but one iridescent
gleam a-week—the Sunday harbour-trip with
their “boy.”
* * #
The girls’ custom was to leave the house
in a bunch after an eight o’clock breakfast of tea
and bread-and-butter—boarders at 12s. 6d. weekly
are not permitted to enjoy other than in imagina-
tion a more substantial matutinal meal—and
together they would pass down Albion-street
towards Belmore Park and so to the city. My
wife and I had frequently watched them from our
balcony across the street to the shady path, and
would speculate on the probable duration of their
friendship, for they were not merely fellow-
boarders, but friends. One morning, however,
four only crossed the thoroughfare. The other
went down the'side on which the sun was already
glaring hotly and distressingly—alone.
“They have quarrelled,” said my wife; “and
■depend upon it that one girl is in the wrong. All
the other four would not have turned against her
•otherwise. ”
The fool that is in every married man, and
which prompts him to occasionally demand free-
dom of speech in his wife’s ear, thereby provoking
for himself grievous ignominy and humiliation,
put it into my head to make a remark. “Now, I
should have thought differently. From my know-
ledge of the sex, I should say that when four
women agree to ‘ cut ’ a sister, the latter is in the
right. Women never appreciate a character which
is a reproach to themselves.”
“Tush!” declared the arbitress of my fate.
“You are speaking from an imperfect knowledge
of women. With this class it is different. They
are nearer to Nature’s heart, notwithstanding
their affectations and follies, and their woman-
hood is purer, simpler, and truer than that of
their superior sisters. Mark my words : there is
something seriously wrong with that girl, or the
others would not have broken off with her, as it
is plain they have. ”
I am the least assertive of men and, therefore,
though I held to my opinion, I said no more.
* * *
We—my wife and I—dined with the girls.
'They had their breakfasts by themselves, and
took their lunches with them—those paltry
lunches !—but at the chief meal, the meat-tea,
they sat down with us.
That evening the four entered the dining-room
in a group. The fifth had not then arrived home,
nor did she come in until the meal was well
advanced. Then she entered, and with a defiant
look at the four, who would not meet her gaze,
took her seat in her usual place, which was next
my wife’s.
And now a curious thing happened. The girl,
who had apparently flung resentment at her co-
workers, and had challenged their scorn, drew
away from ray wife—her cheeks flamed furiously
then a bloodless paleness conquered the red
colour. And, whilst on other nights she had
responded not unintelligently to our conversa-
tional overtures, she now was abrupt to the verge
of rudeness in her one-syllabled replies. As for
the four other girls they, too, were shackled by
restraints of manner and speech. It had been at
once an educative experiment and a pleasure to
observe how their minds had been throwing out
suckers and tendrils of intelligence under the
nurturing influence of converse with people who
knew something, more than they did of the world
outside the cheap boarding-house, the public-
school and the shop ; but to-night there was no
lighting-up of the dull, work-wearied eyes, as
some hitherto undreamt of thought swept into
their mental horizon—no eager beaming of the
face in welcome of the visitant. Instead, there was
blankness, silence, the misery of stupidity.
There was no necessity for my wife and I to
exchange one word as to the meaning of this
alteration of demeanour, or for the girls to take
the former into their confidence. For it was
patent, the significance of it all —the severance in
the morn ng, the antagonism of the night. The
flush on the one girl's cheek was the banneret of
newly-born shame.
* * *
We had just returned to our room, when
a timid, tremulous knock touched the panels of
tne door. I opened it. One of the four stood on
the landing—an angle of light through the stair-
opening of the upper floor showing that the doors
of the girls’rooms were ajar, evidently to permit
their occupants to hear the result of the mission.
\\ould 1 mean—oh, it doesn’t matter—”
the girl confusedly murmured:
* Miss V You wish to see me?”
My wife came to the door, and, thrusting me back
asked of the girl her errand.
‘’ If you would—not mind—just a minute-
askin’ your pardon, ma’am ”
“Of course not—come in here !”
I went down to spend a solitary hour in
the sitting-cwm-difiing-room. I had time to
study every lineament of the cheap oleograph
portrait of Parnell which was the sole pictorial
adornment of the apartment; time to speculate
on how the original maker of the shell-covered
money-box (wholesale Sydney price, Bs. per dozen )
that, from the pride of place on the mantel
symbolised jdie undeveloped artistic cravings and
matured moan economies of the landlady, could
keep body and soul together on the profits of the
manufacture; time to yawn over an Evening
News leader, and to doze over a scintillation of
Echo wit; time to do all these things, before I
was recalled to be brought face to face again with
the eternal terror of the “ social \ roblein.”
* # #
All the live girls had, it seemed, passed
into our room at my wife's invitation, but none of
the four would (at first) say a word beyond—“ Ask
her, ma'am.” As for the fifth girl, she would not
speak, because her shame held her dumb, and had
cut, seemingly, her vocal cords. My wife had to
press them more than once fpr a statement of their
business with her :—“lf yog will not tell me, then
I must ask you to go away, for my husband has
writing to do ! ”
Then it was, that the girl spoke, the girl with
the leprous taint.
“Writin’l” The syllables forced themselves
through a half-strangling sob. “ Writfn’! Why
don’t he write an’ tell the people what things go
on in this city ? Why don’t he write an’ tell them
why poor girls like me don’t— can't— keep-
square?” Then, flinging off some of her shame,
she assumed the heart-breaking brazenness of the
utterly-reckless woman. “ Why we can’t keep
square-let him tell them that! An’ those girls
there, my mates that was, let him ask them why I
didn’t keep square—an’, why, sooner or later,
they JI have to go the same way !”
That last remark was as the turning-on of the
waters. Ihe other four, stung with the taunt,
and righteous with the unstinted wrath of the as
yc untempted woman, abandoned reticence for
voluble speech. They could keep straight—and
why could she not ? They could live upon 15s. a
week-and why shouldn’t she ? They could hold
their tongue when the forewomen were “nasty”
f”. > billets, refuse to give back
lip for the insults of petty tyrants—and why
couldntshe? J
.1 rorn the tangle of confused talk and violent re-
crimination, and tears that hurt more than
poisoned words, my wife at last deduced this
much : that the offending girl, who was an
ea Val°J e ’ t x t } le others, of a large drapery and
outfitting firm, had been dismissed for insolence
the previous Saturday, and that, so far from
seeking for other work, she had done, well—what
would a parentless, workless, friendless girl, who
feared hunger and death more than shame, do?
* * *
“ That is why we told her, ma’am, fco-night
as she was a-goin’ out she shouldn’t come in again,
for we d made up our mind to tell the landlady
said one of the four.
“ We've our characters to lose, we ’ave—an’
we re not willin’ to lose’em for her sake, though we
iU u bin mates ! ” cried another.
“We’d be turned out o’ the shop to-morrow”
saul a third—the one who had come to our door.
ihe square girls wouldn’t work with us, an’ the
girls we suspect of not being straight an’ no better
than she is, would vote with the square ones to
prevent us guessin’ they’re up to the same game '
JNo I—she s got to go or else we will! ”
“The shop’ll turn you out, will they?” the
accused laughed hysterically. “An’ yet they all
heard the forewoman—damn her, I say, I never
said a bad word before, but damn her, damn her
Her passion choked her.
V\ hat did the woman say ? ” pressed my wife
appal led at this glimpse into the volcano of the
girl s heart.
The girl who had been first to speak of the four
answered ;• She told her, Nell there, she’d have
to fine her five shillings for bad work, or some-
thing*
“ For badly finishin’ a skirt! ”
\ es, that was it. But, of course, it wasn’t
true—we know that.”
’ Not true,” breathed rhy wife.
“ No-never is true ! The truth was she’d had
a bad week—y know the forewoman of each room
has to turn out so many costumes a-week, or make
so much profit some other way, or else she won’t
get her premium from the bosses.”
“ Premium ? What is that ? ”
“ If she makes so much profit over a certain
sum, she gets a percentage over her salary. So
her object is to cut down expenses, an’ pay the
work-girls less. An’ she can nearly always show
time' ” * nm em ’ or ma kin’ ’em work over-
“ Is this true ? ”
, f m I ri j e • ” exclaimed the girl with the taint—
line . It sas true as Gospel, or as that I ain’t
square no longer. That’s true enough, God
knows!” She turned her head away, and her
light form swayed with the tumult of her sound*
Jess sobs. The four mates looked on her humilia-
tion stonily.
|| And she fined you ? ” , ,
Yes, fined me five-bob 1 I asked her howl
hve on A ten shillings—l was supposed to

as the other girls did to make it up ! ”

and you won t have pluck to answer her as I did!”
What did you say ? ”
ber I would do as she meant, and
that the sin of it, an’ the shame of it, should be on
her an the men, her bosses. I told her that, an’,
of course, I was sacked on the spot! ”
Poor thing, poor girl!” The hand of the
elder woman touched the stricken girl pityingly
but it was jerked aside.
want none of your pity ! You ladies
,e to blame for it all. You pay your two guineas
or your three guineas ;to the front-shop, for makin’
your costumes, an’ you never ask how much we
get out of it—you don’t choose to think that the

In her tragic arraignment of Fate she erred
somewhat. But, as my wife questioned, “ Was
she far wrong ?” ■ .
* * •
She passed into the sultry night—she
brushed the hands that would have withheld her
aside as though they had not more than the
retaining strength of cobwebs. And as for the-
merciless four who yet were so wise in the know-
ledge of their world—well, they are only three-
t£-Ti' , ln another year how many will there be?'
Will there be another girl’s soul waiting to be
entered to the debit of the great Sydney firm’s
profit and loss account, which records, on the
credit side, the gain on No. 3 work-room’s output
for the year ? Who will undertake to say No ?
PRICE WARUNG.
Ebenezer Ward, M.L.C. (S.A.), is said to
have stated on his Supreme Court oath that ho
never told a lie in his life.” Ebenezer Washing-
ton Ward. p
The corpses of Chinese who die in America
are returned to the Flowery Land in mutilated
fragments tinned-up after the fashion of canned
meats. A freight of supposed dead-Chinaman
recently arrived in China from ’Frisco, and was
found by the sorrowing relatives to be a consign-
ment of tinned American-hog and preserved fish
which should have gone to Europe. The infer-
ence is that the canned-Chinee cargo has been
sent by mistake to Europe, and has been con-
sumed unawares by the retail purchasers as some
of America’s export delicacies.

The Governor of Tasmania (Sir Robert
Hamilton), in a speech made at a public dinner in
Hobart, stated that the culpable mismanagement
of the smashed-up Bank of Van Diemen’s Land
ought to be searchingly inquired into. So it
ought, but tremendous efforts are made to stifle
inconvenient enquiries.
The S.M. Herald is a strictly “ constitu"
tional ” paper; but its afternoon edition, the
Echo, writes leading articles in favour of lynch
law. Lynch law for a penny ; constitutionalism
for tuppence. By the way, the Echo aforesaid is
conveniently oblivious. The Bulletin doesn’t
object to its scissoring ’Frisco Argonaut whole-
sale and promptly. It only suggests that the Echo
should acknowledge when it steals. Or, perhaps
a less troublesome way, it should label “Echo”
what it doesn't crib.
Rev. Yair, a Scotch parson, now aged 91,
proved himself in his youth a clerical Lochinvar.
The parents of the lady to whom he was attached
were so much against the match, that when the
wedding-morning arrived, they securely locked
their daughter in Her bed-room. Mr. Yair, how-
ever, humbly pleaded that he might at least be
allowed to shake hands with the young lady, and
bid her good-bye. His request was granted, and
the prisoner was brought out. Then the bride-
groom’s chance came, Seizing the girl by the
right hand, he said, “I take you to be my wife.”
The blushing bride, as swiftly replied, “ I take
you to be my husband; ” and the wedding was
complete—according to the Scotch law. Then the
Reverend Joseph carried off his bride, before the
eyes of the dumbfounded parents.
The grim and gruesome French murder is
a standing subject for the moral British paper to
preach about, and the innate brutality of the
French criminal is much howled over. Here, as a
contrast, is a nice, fresh (by last mail) British
murder —a bright and cheerful homicide with a lot
of thrill in it. One of the criminals was eight
years old —he was addicted to going out at night,
and his mother locked him up in the evenings and
confiscated his clothes so as to make him stay in
bed. He got riled at this, and thought it would
be a handy thing to murder another boy and keep
his togs for emergencies. The other small
homicide aged nine—joined in the murder
merely for the sake of amusement. He was a
blase, haggard, dissipated small boy who
wanted some new excitement. They selected
a child of eight years, and beguiled him
to a lonely, unfinished building, and threw him
down 12 feet into a pool of water. Apparently
they had counted on the fall killing him, but as
he wasn’t injured they hauled him up, and having
stripped him naked they threw him in again. The
miserable infant, who was thus being soaked and
concussed to death, still objected, so he was
(hauled out once more. Then the pair concluded
(that a longer drop might do him good, and this
■time they threw him a distance of 16 feet, after
which they climbed down and sat on his head to
ikeep him under water. To make quite sure, in
fact, they sat on his head off and on for about two
(hours after he was dead. Finally they annexed
his clothes and went home. They are both quite
happy over the event.

On Friday evening, 30th October, ' 189],
Sydney Domain was infested with immense hordes
of the most destructive kind of flying termites
(white ants). On a November evening in 1833, -a
similar phenomenon was witnessed. A convict- ,
woman got a ticket-of-leave for running into the
midst of the myriad-thronged insects, and saying
a child upon whose face and body they had settled
in thousands.
Brisbane brewers are now cut-throating
each other in business. Until recently, they had
formed a ring, the rule of which was that they
sold beer to the publicans at <£4 per hogshead. A
split in the syndicate occurred the other day r and
an enterprising firm cut down the price to £2 10s., J
whereupon another firm “went one better” by
distributing its beer at £2 per hogshead—
HE LIGHTS HIS PIPE ONCE MORE. !
Some fifty years ago the English ship “Argos*
was wrecked on a low-lying key or sand island of
the Bahama group. Only one man, a sailor, wap-
thrown safely on the beach by the waves. In his
pocket was a tin tobacco-box, a pipe, and a flint
and steel. Wringing the water from his clothes,
he seated himself, lighted his pipe, and with true
British phlegm proceeded to. consider his situa-
tion. The moral of which is that when an English-
man, having the means, refuses to smoke, he is in
very bad form.
For example, here is a man who says, “I always
enjoyed my pipe, but now I couldn’t take a whiff,-’
To be sure, there was a reason, and he puts it in
this way. “Up to Michaelmas, 1887,” he says;
“ I was a strong, healthy man. About this time
I began to feel bad at my stomach and had no
relish for food. I had a bad taste in the mouth,
and after eating I would retch and vomit until,
the water oozed out of my eyes; and so bad was
this that my wife had to hold my head. My eyes
turned yellow, and I felt low, weak, and nervous.
Sometimes I would break out into a sweat and
then go cold and chilly.
“ I couldn’t touch solid food, and for months I
belched up sour water; and what I did eat lay
heavy upon, and seemed to be dead and lifeless in
my body. Feeling low and depressed, I had ho
enjoyment in company. x .
“ Before this I was always cheerful and enjoyed
my pipe, hut now I couldn’t take a tvh iff.
“I had a craving, gnawing pain at the stomach,
which nothing eased for long together. I had
bran poultices applied, and took different kinds of
medicine, but nothing went to the spot, nothing
eased me. After a time I had to give up my work,
for I got so weak and nervous / couldn’t hit my
work iron, and my tools and th ings flew out of my
hand.
“ For over four months I did not have a single
good night’s rest. I would turn and turn in bed
all nigfht long, and my wife and I often sat up the
best part of the night rather than go to, bed., I
went so thin that my clothes fairly hung upon me!
Friends who came to see me told one another I
could not get better, and even my wife said I
would do no more work in this world.
“ For more than twelve months I had a clever
doctor attending me, but his medicine did me no
good; then I went to see a physician at Sudbury,
with the same result. The doctors sounded my
lungs, but found nothing wrong, and said all my
ailments were brought on by bad digestion.
“ I now got weaker and weaker, and had given
up all hopes of recovery, when in the spring of
1889 a lady from London who was staying at the
vicarage at Otten Belchamp heard of my condi-
tion. She called at Mr. Goody’s, the medicine
dealer, and told him to supply me with some
Mother Siegel’s Curative Syrup and she would
pay for it. Very reluctantly 1 took the medi-
cine, for I had tried so many things without
obtaining any benefit. However, my wife pressed
me so hard that at last I began to take the Syrup.
After a few doses I said to my wife, ‘ I think I
feel better for this medicine,’ and from that time I
commenced to improve. By the time I had taken
three bottles I was back at my work, as strong
and well as ever—much to the surprise of every-
body.
“ People all about said I would never get locll,
hut I did, thank the Lord.
“Now I tell everyone that Mother Seigel’s
Syrup saved my life. I can now eat anything,
and feel so light-hearted I could jump over a five-
barred gate. My neighbours all say I look ten
years younger.”
Elias Bland, shoemaker,
Belchamp St. Paul, Clare, Suffolk.
The doctors who attributed Mr. Bland’s illness
to indigestion and dyspepsia were quite right.
All they lacked w r as the proper remedy. This
appeared in Mother Seigel’s Syrup, and now our
good friend enjoys his pipe as he did in other
years. If he is ever handled in that way again we
may wager he won’t forget what to do.

Every man his own hangman. The
Yankees have invented a gallows so contrived
that, when the victim steps on the platform he sets
free a jet of water which by releasing a weight
jerks him in the air.
Apropos the Mortiraer-Granville alcohol
discussion “ The proposition connecting total
abstainers with exceptional longevity,” remarks a
London doctor, “always assumes the form that
total abstinence is the cause of this effect. My
professional experience does not warrant the pro-
position in this shape. Their longevity, as a
whole, is admitted; total abstinence, however, is
not the sole cause; that cause consists of a
generally prudential character of which total ab-
stinence is merely the exhibition in one particular
direction.”

According to the Times correspondent at
Berlin, a leading German military critic says that
if France and Germany fight, “the nation to be
beaten first will be the nation which goes to attack
the other.”
An Anglican bishop vouches for the fact
that, at a recent clerical examination, a candidate
for holy orders wrote that (the regicide) Oliver
Cromwell, on his death-bed, cried : “ If I had
served my God as I served my King, I should
not be thus forsaken in my old age ! ”
N.S.W. Labour-member Rae’s remark
that he had invested in another new shirt so as t°
give the laundresses a turn, reminds us that there
is in Queensland a now rather wealthy lawyer
.whom we only met on two occasions. The first
time he was waiting in bed waiting for his
only shirt, which was being washed by a lady,
locally known as the “Bull-pup.” The second
time he was leading to the altar the daughter of
a mining millionaire.
Hugh Gilmore, the Adelaide Socialist
clergyman, a man of remarkable oratorical and con-
versational powers, died the other day, after suffer-
ing dreadful agony, from internal cancer. Thirty
years ago he was a homeless wandering street
arab in Glasgow, and had it not been for a charit-
able schoolmaster would probably have never lived
to preach the “ national ” gospel. Gilmore’s
advent to South Australia boomed Radicalism in
a remarkable way. He was a Primitive Methodist,
but declined to confine his religion to any creed,
and was ever at the head of any movement
calculated in his opinion to raise the standard of
human happiness. His loss to the S.A. demo-
cratic party is almost irreparable.
J. J. Dodd, Mayor of Scone (N.S.W.),
also District-Secretary to the Pai\toralists’ Union,
when officially “ requested ” to resign either that
position or his J.P.-ship, sweetly “fails to see
how the two positions are incompatible,” and,
“ not being a paid officer under the Government,”
he “ declines to resign either ” honour. This is a
petty matter, anyhow, but the objection sounds
queerly coming from the ranks of those who fought
against unionists being enrolled as special con-
stables, and laughed to scorn the idea of labour-
leaders being appointed to the Commission of the
Peace. It is the same old special claim of special
privilege. The outward and visible signs of law
are only for the pastoralist and the man of Pro-
perty. Packed benches are the prerogatives of
men with “ stakes in the country.”

The Theatre Royal Hotel, Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars has been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange, curious
and refreshing drinks known in the United States
can be obtained. Their name is legion, but among
them may be mentioned champagne cocktails,
gin fizz, milk punch, mint julep—probably the
noblest liquor known to man—imperial punch,
Manhattan cocktails, and many others. All
mixed by American bar-keeps. James A. Walsh,
proprietor, late of the Occidental Hotel, San
Francisco.*

A German physician of great insightedness
defines the main dif. in the effects of whisky and
of beer as follows : “ Visky makes you kill some-
body else; mit beer you only kills yourself.” Pass
both of them.
It occurred thus. Mr. and Mrs. Buffles
put up for the night at a Charters Towers (N.Q.)
hotel. Buffles was badly attacked in the night
with spasms, and Mrs. B. rushed out for a doctor,
but rouse one up. Then she remembered
some-mustard-leaves she had in a hand-bag in the
sitting-room. She procured one and returned
up-stairs. To her astonishment, Buffles was
placidly asleep. But she wasn’t going to be
fooled out of her trouble, so she gently applied
the mustard-leaf where it ought to do most good,
and sat down by the bedside to await results. She
didn’t have to wait long. Buffles moved uneasily
once or twice, and then sat up with a yell that
made Mrs. Buffles’ blood freeze. Piercingly she
screamed, and Charters Towers became alive with
snowy robes of night and jack-o’-lanterns. Among
the foremost to enter the apartment was—horror !
Buffles in his pyjamas! Buffles with—“By the
great platterpuss of the Hockingjock Ranges!
Give me gore and a sponge. My wife in a strange
man’s bedroom ! Oh, my connubial felicity !
Come on, 'everybody, and see me die, martyred
by a woman’s frailty.” Mrs. Buffles had mistaken
the bedroom and plastered the wrong man’s
diaphragm. It took fourteen affidavits and a
jug of whisky to convince Buffles of his wife’s
innocence.
Extract from an offi ;ial despatch by James
Russell Lowell; —
One of the dev ices of Fourcarde which came within M.
Silvelo’s own knowledge when in another department of
the Government is so ingenious and amusing as to be
worth recounting. The Frenchman’s object was to
smuggle petroleum into Madrid without paying the
octroi. To this end he established storehouses in the
suburbs, and then, hiring all the leanest and least mam-
malian women that could be found, he made good all
their physical defects with tin cases filled with petroleum,
thus giving them what Dr. Johnson would have called the
pectoral proportions of Juno. Doubtless he blasphemed
the unwise parsimony of Nature in denying to women in
general the multitudinous breasts displayed by certain
Hindoo idols. For some time these seemingly milky
mothers passed without question into the unsuspecting
city, and supplied thousands of households with that
cheap enlightenment which cynics say is worse than none.
Meanwhile, M. Fourcarde’s pockets swelled in exact
proportion to the Quaker breastworks of the improvised
wet-nurses. - Could he only have been moderate ! Could
he only have bethought him in time of the ne quid nimis !
But one fatal day he sent in a damsel whose contours
aroused in one of the guardians at the gates the same
emotions as those of Maritornes in the bosom of the
carrier. With the playful gallantry of a superior he
tapped the object of his admiration, and it tinkled. He
had “struck oil” unawares. Love shook his wings and
fled. Duty retired frowning, and M. Fourcarde’s peram-
bulating wells suddenly went dry.
Dear Bulletin, —I noticed a paragraph in
The Australasian of October 10, re a paper read
before the geographical section of the British
Association by a Miss Clarke, She cited the
“Spanish Benedictine Monastery at New Norcia,
80 miles from Perth, W. A., as the most striking
refutation of the generally received belief in the
irredeemable degradation of the Australian abo-
rigines.” In the early part of this year I passed
through New Norcia with a mob of horses, and
took special notice of the educated black. In
common with most Queenslanders I believed that
once a nigger knows too much he ceases to be
useful to himself or anybody else, and what I saw
there only tended to strengthen that opinion.
They (the nigs.) are put up in cottages, each of
which has what has been intended for a garden in
front, and when any of the monks are about their
behaviour is good, even pious ; but let the good
monk turn his back and King Tarpot is once more
himself —can rip round, and swear generally, and
conduct himself as a blackfellow should. The
gins are the same as in other parts of Australia,
for, though correctly churched and living in an
outwardly respectable manner with their black
husbands in suburban villas, none of them are
virtuous to the verge of eccentricity. New Norcia
is a novel sight to a native-born Australian. The
buildings are half Australian and half Moorish in
style. The monks, who wear long beards, are
clothed in sombre gowns, and have a most
gruesome appearance. The sight of them
made a Murchison River black I had with
me turn almost pale. Each monk has a dif-
ferent trade, and they can turn out anything,
from a linoh-pin to a landau. But the chapel is
the centre of attraction ; at the most inopportune
times a deep-sounding bell booms, and they all
cease work and make tracks for the sacred edifice,
presumably for prayer. The nigs, don’t join in
these promiscuous devotions, no doubt reserving
themselves for a good blow-out on Sunday. The
Benedictines are past-masters in the game of land-
grab, for they have secured a good-sized run, the
outside blocks of which are fenced at each corner,
ready for a continuation should any democratic
Government imagine it open for selection.
In this they have been aided by a large store-
keeper, who held a position under the old regime.
They bought his goods, and he obtained their
freeholds. They receive from a maternal Govern-
ment £15 for every nigger they bring into the
fold, and certainly knock a little work out of
them. The nigger rides your buckjumper, the
monk takes the money. Where the profits of this
self-supporting and no-wage establishment go is
not known, but no doubt the terrible Infanta of
Spain or some other superfluous person draws a
div. New Norcia is the first civilised part that
Ernest Giles struck after one of his exploring
trips. The few settlers round about greatly
admire this vast Ultra-conservative monkery, but
where white people won’t trouble about obtaining
a vote, call the boss “the master,” scrub “a
thicket,” and a paddock a “field,” they are only
in the polyp stage of existence, and one can afford
to look on them with feelings of pity and sym-
pathy. The monks drive as hard a bargain as any
Jew, but, to their credit be it said, no man
travelling per boot knocks at the door in vain for
a feed.—Yours, Dan. Turnback, Snowy River,
21st Oct., ’91.
“Nursing is one of the deadliest occupa-
tions known to women. More than half the-
deaths of Catholic nurses in 38 German hospitals',
were due to tuberculosis. A healthy girl of
seventeen devoting herself to hospital-nursing dies-
on an average twenty-one and a half years sooner
than a girl of the same age in the general popula-
tion. A woman of twenty-four will live twenty-
two years longer in the outside population than
what she would do if she were a nurse in a
hospital.”
A good many years ago, when Editor
MTlwraith first settled in Rockhampton (Q.) and
started to run the Morning Bulletin in that
region, he had an interesting experience with the
local hospital. There was an old-established feud
of some kind between the paper and tiro hospital,
and, therefore, all the advertising went to
MTlwraith's loathsome contemporary across the
street. When the new proprietary had settled
down, however, and got a firm hold of things, its
gentlemanly representative waited on the hospital
authorities, and suggested that the hatchet might
be buried with advantage; but iie was received
with such unnecessary contumely that he went
back and reported the circumstance to his em-
ployer. That astute old man was writing
a leader on the dairying industry or some-
thing, but he stopped and listened, and smiled
a heavenly smile inside his long white beard.
“Bide a wee, mon, till wo get into the new
oaffice,” he said, placidly, and the canvasser
“bided” accordingly. They got into the new
office, and then ho called again, and was received
in a more hostile manner even than before. He
came back and reported once more. “ It’s all
right, mon,’’said the MTlwraith, gently ; “bide
a wee longer till we get settled down in the new
oaffice.” It took him about thre*e months to settle
down to his own satisfaction. Then one day he
sent along a letter, pointing out that, according
to the statute, all advertisements were to b e
inserted in the nearest newspaper, and en-
closing a surveyor’s certificate, which testified
that since MTlwraith had got into the new
oaffice, his paper was just two feet nine inches
nearer than the other one. He also appended
a memo about the tines due for three months
continuous disregard of the statute; and when
the hospital committee saw the total, it gave a
yell and fled tumultuously. Next day the adver-
tising business was shifted to MTlwraith’s oaffice,
and the old man kindly said nothing more about
the fines. But the committee has treated him
with great respect ever since.
Johnnie Livingston, a well-known
celebrity, who .lives chiefly on grievances, peti-
tioned the Tasmanian Parliament lately for
redress against a wily old lawyer, who, (Johnnie
says) sold him seme land one week, and re-sold it
to another Johjmie a fortnight after. Bub this
took place forty years ago ! and as everybody who
knows anything; about it is dead and buried, the
House declined to call witnesses.
“Did you ever see a lame Chinaman?”
asks a Charters Towers paper, and proceeds to
give the reason why you never did. Because,
when a Chinaman gets badly hurt and loses a
limb, his mates kill him. ’Chat is a part of their
religion, it is added. If so, The Bulletin
considers that that part of the Chinese religion
should, become, statutorily established, that
rewards should be offered for maiming Chinamen,
and that State-school children should be en-
couraged to study Chinese anatomy. A Pigtail
Day might be added to the list of holidays as a
healthy set-off against Arbor Day ; it might be
; styled Barber Day. ‘ 4 1 know a Chinaman,” said a
Winton (Q.) man, recently, “ who got hurt while
; ilasting rock on a railway-works. His left hand
vas blown off and his right was left. A doctor
amputated the arm and fixed him up. Wo missed
,he Chinaman a few days later, and in answer to
■ inquiry, his friends said that he was dead.
Died of the. wound, eh?’ ‘No; dlinkee tea,
.allee same something in it —dlinkee, killee him.’ ”
Once more, did you ever see a lame Chinaman ?
Pastor Fitchott, late of Melbourne D.T.
{and —ahem ! —the Wesleyan Young Ladies’Col-
lege). is trying to make out in the Pall Mall
Gazette that there is no nationalistic feeling in
Australia, and that the people’s sentiment is
even more imperialistic than public opinion in
Great Britain itself. He is “confident that in the
event of the Empire being threatened with disaster
the colonies would leap to the side of the mothe r
land, and stand ready to pour out their blood and
treasure in its cause.'’ M’yes! The Bulletin,
which lie unsparingly abuses, evidently stinks in
Fitchett’s nostrils, and he asserts that the “great
weeklies” —names, please—of Australia “taken
together, have 500 readers where The Bulletin
has one.” Fitohett, who is a clever writer (for a
parson), needn’t descend to barefaced lies to bolster
up his craze. His conscious mendacities, could
he confine their circulation to England, might be
accepted, but when read in Australia, they only
expose him to derision.
A Melbourne medical student has just
been rusticated for twelve months, for cribbing
at exam.
“ Tlib World’s Fair” pops up now as a
frequent heading in the cablegram column. Tile
cable liar is at it again. The world couldn’t be
fair if it tried.
Admiral Paris, Director of the Marine
Museum at the Louvre, in a recent interview,
mentioned that in 1831. when he belonged to the
expedition sent in search of La Perouse, they
arrived off the coast of Australia, dying the white
flag of France powdered witli fleur-de-lis, and it
was only in Sydney harbour that they learned the
news of the fall of Charles X. and the revolution,
a year after the event.
